Conditions for the Hadd of Theft
A Hanafi commentary enumerating conditions for the hadd of theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven conditions required for the hadd of theft and apply them to determine whether amputation is obligatory.

Core Idea
The theft amputation penalty is only applied when all conditions are met: hirz, secrecy, valid ownership, non-perishable property, dar al-adl, and no shubha.

In Hanafi fiqh, the severity of the hadd punishment for theft demands that every condition be precisely satisfied. This passage enumerates seven essential conditions that must be present before the penalty can be applied, and explains why the locus (mahall) of the act is legally considered a condition. Understanding these conditions is critical to correctly applying the law and avoiding errors in judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the lesson concerns the crime of theft (sariqa) and the prescribed hadd penalty (amputation). The conditions are prerequisites for the penalty, not for the act being sinful.
2
Enumerate the seven conditions
From the passage, list each condition: (1) property is valuable (mutaqawwim), (2) taken from a safe place (hirz), (3) taken secretly (not open force), (4) victim has a valid hand (ownership and possession), (5) property not quickly perishable, (6) theft occurs in a land of justice (dar al-adl), (7) no shubha (doubt or ambiguity).
3
Distinguish condition from cause
The text explains why the locus is a condition (shart) and not a cause (sabab). A condition is external to the act and does not bring it about, whereas a cause leads to and has an effect on the act.
4
Analyze the exclusions
For each condition, note the exclusion that prevents the hadd: e.g., swallowing a dinar (lack of being apparent), raiding (lack of secrecy), theft from a thief (no valid hand), perishable goods, dar al-bagh/harb (not dar al-adl), non-hirz, and cases with shubha.
5
Compare with opposing school
Briefly note that some Shafi'i scholars disagree on the conditions of absolute valuation and dar al-adl. The Hanafi position is outlined here.

The Book of Theft: Its pillar (rukn), its locus (mahall), its nisab (minimum threshold), and its ruling (hukm). It is a condition. [2] (It is a condition): For the locus of the act is a condition for the act because it is external to the act yet needed by it. And by the restriction of (the property being) apparent (as a condition), it excludes the case where a person swallows a dinar in a safe place and then leaves: he is not amputated. And by the restriction of secrecy (as a condition), it excludes taking by way of raid and overpowering. And by the restriction that the person from whom it is stolen has a valid hand (ownership), it excludes theft from the thief; there is no amputation in that case. And by his statement: 'something that does not quickly become corrupt', it excludes theft of something that spoils in a short time; there is no amputation. And by his statement: 'in a land of justice', it excludes theft in a land of rebellion or a land of war. And by his statement: 'from a safe place', it excludes theft from a place that is not safe. And by his statement: 'there is no doubt in it nor interpretation', it excludes theft with doubt or interpretation. (1) (2) This is a summary of what the author of 'al-Bahr', 'al-Fath', and 'al-Bada'i' and others mentioned. Also added is the restriction that the property must be valuable absolutely, i.e., according to the people of every religion; thus there is no amputation for the theft of wine of a Muslim. [1] His statement: 'It is a condition': meaning that the stolen property being valuable, secured, and owned is a condition for the act of theft, because theft is not legally realized except with it. [2] His statement: 'For the locus... etc.' is a reasoning for it being a condition. Its apparent meaning is that the locus itself is a condition, or it can be said: the locus being described with those qualities is a condition, as we have indicated. The gist is that the locus of the act is external to the act, and the act needs it, so it is a condition for it. If you say: The mere fact that something is external and needed is not sufficient for it to be a condition, because this exists also in the cause (sabab). I answer: The cause leads to the act and has an effect in bringing it about in some way, whereas the condition does not lead to it. It is known that the locus of the act does not lead to it, so it is nothing but a condition. (1) 'al-Bahr al-Raiq' (5:55-56). (2) 'Fath al-Qadir' (5:354-355). [PAGE_274] 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. And because of the obviousness of this matter, the commentator did not mention it, so the objection against him that his reasoning proves something more general than what is claimed is refuted. Without it.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions (shurut) required for the hadd punishment of theft (amputation of the hand) in Hanafi fiqh. The commentator begins by stating that the act of theft has a pillar (rukn), a locus (mahall), a nisab (minimum value), and a ruling. He then explains that the locus being a condition is proven by the fact that it is external to the act yet necessary for its existence. The conditions are then enumerated: the property must be taken from a safe place (hirz), the taking must be secret (not open force), the victim must have a valid hand over the property (ownership and possession), the property must not be perishable quickly, the theft must occur in a land of justice (not dar al-harb or baghi), and there must be no doubt or ambiguity (shubha). Additionally, the property must be valuable (mutaqawwim) according to the religion of the owner; thus, wine belonging to a Muslim is not considered valuable, so no amputation for stealing it. The commentator then addresses a potential objection that the reasoning (ta'lil) might prove too much, but he defends the original point by noting that the matter is obvious and the commentator omitted it. This passage is a typical Hanafi expansion on the conditions of theft, ensuring that the hadd is applied only when all conditions are fulfilled.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is normally kept protected.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity regarding the permissibility of taking the property, which prevents the hadd.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that is considered valuable and legally permissible according to Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi conditions stated, which of the following would NOT be punishable by amputation?
A) Stealing a gold ring from a locked drawer in a Muslim land.
B) Taking money from a friend's wallet while they sleep, with no permission.
C) Seizing goods openly in a raid during war (dar al-harb).
D) Taking a sheep from a field that is not enclosed, at night.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft is only applied when the property is valuable, from a safe place, taken secretly, owned by a valid possessor, not perishable, in a land of justice, and with no ambiguity.

The Nisab for Theft Amputation: The Minimum Value
Explanation of the nisab (10 dirhams or 1 dinar) with hadith proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi nisab for theft amputation and explain the hadith proof that establishes it.

Core Idea
No amputation for theft unless the stolen value is ten dirhams or one dinar.

The hadd punishment for theft is one of the most severe penalties in Islamic law, so it is restricted by strict conditions. The most important condition is the nisab—the minimum value of stolen goods. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that the nisab is ten dirhams of silver or one dinar of gold, supported by explicit hadiths and contrasted with views of other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Element
The nisab is a condition (shart) for applying the hadd of amputation. Determine what the Hanafi school considers the minimum stolen value that triggers the punishment.
The nisab is not a rukn; it is an external condition.
2
Locate the Primary Dalil
The central proof is the hadith: 'لا تقطع اليد إلا في دينار أو عشرة دراهم' (The hand is not cut except for a dinar or ten dirhams). Identify that this is a sunnah ahad transmission from Ibn Mas'ud.
Also supporting narrations about the shield valued at ten dirhams.
3
Analyse the Wajh al-Dalalah
The word 'إلا' (except) indicates exclusivity (hasr). This means cutting is limited to these amounts, establishing that the nisab is exactly ten dirhams or one dinar.
The proof is qati in dalala (clear meaning) though zanni in thubut.
4
Note Inter-Madhhab Ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school interprets the same hadith differently, setting the nisab at three dirhams based on another narration. Other schools have varying thresholds.
The Hanafi response relies on the strength of multiple corroborating reports.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi due to multiple authentic hadiths and the practice of the Companions. The later Hanafi masters uphold this as the relied-upon ruling.
The shield incident (mijann) provides a concrete historical example of the nisab.

And its nisab: the amount of ten. [2] 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. Without it. [1] His statement: 'And its nisab': i.e., the amount for which amputation is obligatory, and for what is above it, and it is not obligatory for what is below it. [2] His statement: 'the amount of ten dirhams': he indicated by this that what is considered from silver is ten dirhams, and from other than it its equivalent. The basis for this is the hadith: 'The hand is not cut except for a dinar or ten dirhams', narrated by 'Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf from Ibn Mas'ud, marfu'an. Similarly, al-Tabarani, Ahmad in his Musnad, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, Ibn Abi Shaybah, and others narrated it. And al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar narrated from Ibn 'Abbas and 'Abdullah ibn 'Amr ibn al-'As: 'The value of the shield (mijann) for which the Messenger of Allah cut off (the thief's hand) was ten dirhams.' And he narrated from Ayman who said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'The minimum for which a thief's hand is cut off is the price of the shield.' He said: 'And it was valued at that time as a dinar.' And he narrated from Umm Ayman, may Allah be pleased with her, she said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'The hand of a thief is not cut off except for a juhfah (an old shield), and it was valued at the time of the Messenger of Allah as a dinar or ten dirhams.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage explains the nisab (minimum value) required for the hadd punishment of theft. The commentator clarifies that the nisab is ten dirhams (silver coins) or their equivalent in other valuables, which is also one dinar (gold coin). The ruling is based on several hadiths: a marfu' hadith from Ibn Mas'ud stating 'the hand is not cut except for a dinar or ten dirhams'; a narration about the shield (mijann) valued at ten dirhams at the time of the Prophet; and a hadith from Umm Ayman specifying the same threshold. The Hanafi school holds that the nisab is a fixed monetary value, and amputation applies only when the stolen amount reaches or exceeds that value. This is a key condition to prevent hasty application of the severe hadd punishment.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum value of stolen property required to apply the hadd of amputation.
درهم (dirham): A silver coin used as currency; the standard measure for nisab.
دينار (dinar): A gold coin; one dinar is equivalent to ten dirhams in the nisab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum value of stolen property required for amputation of the hand in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Three dirhams.
B) Ten dirhams or one dinar.
C) Five dirhams.
D) One dirham.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets the nisab for theft amputation at ten dirhams of silver or one dinar of gold, based on authentic hadiths that limit the punishment to these amounts.

The Nisab for Theft: Defending the 10-Dirham Threshold
Defense of the Hanafi 10-dirham nisab with responses to three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi defense of the 10-dirham nisab for theft by applying the principles of taking the greater certain amount (al-akhdhu bil-akthar al-mutaqayyan) and warding off punishments by doubts (dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat).

Core Idea
The Hanafi 10-dirham nisab is upheld by preferring certainty and averting penalties through doubt.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defends the Hanafi position on the nisab for theft against three major objections. It demonstrates how Hanafi usul principles are applied to reconcile conflicting hadiths and uphold the 10-dirham threshold. By studying this, you will see how the maxim 'ward off hudud by doubts' operates in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Recognize that the dispute is over the minimum value (nisab) of stolen property that triggers hand amputation.
2
Examine the Hanafi evidence
The text cites hadiths specifying ten dirhams as the price of a shield at the Prophet's time.
These hadiths are from various sources.
3
Understand the three objections
The text lists three objections: weak chains, ahad hadiths restricting the Qur'an, and contradiction with stronger hadiths.
4
Analyze the Hanafi responses
For each objection, the author provides a response: weakness compensated by multiple paths, the existence of nisab is mutawatir, and when amounts conflict take the greater certain amount and apply dar' al-hudud.
The third response is twofold.
5
Determine the tarjih and practical consequence
The Hanafi position is that the hadd is only for 10 dirhams or more, based on the certain amount and shubha for lesser amounts.
This differs from other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة ركنها ومحلها ونصابها وحكمها
ومثله أخرجه النسائي وأبو داود والحاكم عن ابن عباس، وأخرج النسائي عن عبد الله بن عمرو بن العاص قال: كان ثمن المجن على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عشرة دراهم. وأخرج ابن أبي شيبة عن رجل من مزينة مرفوعاً: «ما بلغ ثمن المجنّ قطعت يدُ صاحبه، وكان ثمن المجن عشرة دراهم». وفي الباب أخبار أخر أوردناها في التعليق الممجّد على موطأ محمد. واعترض هنا لوجوه: الأول: إن أخبار التقدير بعشرة دراهم أسانيدها ضعيفة، فلا ينبغي أن يعتبر هنا. وجوابه: إنه ليست جميع أسانيد جميع هذه الأخبار ضعيفة، مع أن الضعف ينجبر بكثرة الطرق. الثاني: إنّ هذه أخبار آحاد، وتقييد مطلق الكتاب، يعني قوله: وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا بها، خلافُ الأصول. وجوابه: إن الآية وإن كانت مطلقة دالة على وجوب القطع في مطلق السرقة، وبه قالت الظاهرية، إلا أنه قد ثبت تقييدها بما تواتر من الأحاديث، فإنّ الأحاديث الدالة على أنه لا يقطعُ في كلّ سرقة، بل له نصاب بلغت مبلغ التواتر، وإن كان خصوص الأحاديث الواردة في التقدير بالعشرة أو بما دونها أحاداً. وبالجملة؛ ليس هاهنا تقييد القطع بالظني، ونسخ الإطلاق القرآني بأخبار آحاد، بل تقييد القطع بالقطعي، فإنّ القدر المشترك من الأحاديث الدالة على التقييد متواتر. الثالث: إنّ أخبار التقدير بالعشرة معارضة بما هو أصح منها، فقد أخرج مالك والبخاري ومسلم وغيرهم، عن ابن عمر: إنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قطع في ثمن مجنّ قيمته ثلاثة دراهم، وأخرج البخاري ومسلم أيضاً مرفوعاً: «لا يقطع السارق إلا في ربع دينار»، وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة مخرجة في السنن والمسانيد، وبها احتج الشافعي ومالك وغيرهما. وجوابه: من وجهين: أحدهما: إنّ الأحاديث لما تعارضت في مقدار ما يقطع فيه السارق، أخذنا بالأكثر المتيقن، وتركنا الأقل الغير المتيقن. وثانيهما: إن أخبار التقدير بالعشرة وإن كان أكثرها ضعيفاً أورثت شبهة في وجوب القطع في أقل من عشرة، وقد ثبت درء الحدود بالشبهات على ما مر فدرءنا الحد عمّن سرق أقل من العشرة، وأوجبناه على من سرقها أو ما فوقها. وبهذا ظهر لك دفع ما طعن به العوام على الحنفية من أنهم تركوا في هذا الباب العمل بأحاديث الصحيحين، وهما أصح الكتب بعد كتاب الله، وأخذوا بأخبار ضعيفة، فاحفظ هذا فإنّه ينفعك في الدينا وفي الآخرة.
Book of Theft: Its pillar, its object, its nisab, and its ruling. Similarly, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, and al-Hakim narrated it from Ibn Abbas, and al-Nasa'i narrated from Abdullah ibn Amr ibn al-As who said: "The price of a shield at the time of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was ten dirhams." Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from a man of Muzayna as a marfu' hadith: "Whatever reaches the price of a shield, the hand of its owner is cut off, and the price of the shield was ten dirhams." There are other reports in this chapter which we have cited in al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad. Objections are raised here on several grounds: First: The reports specifying ten dirhams have weak chains of transmission, so they should not be considered. Its answer: Not all the chains of all these reports are weak, and weakness is compensated by the multiplicity of paths. Second: These are individual reports (akhbar ahad), and restricting the absolute text of the Book, i.e., His statement "And the thief, male and female, cut off their hands," with them is contrary to principles. Its answer: Although the verse is absolute, indicating the obligation of cutting for any theft, and the Zahiris hold this, it has been established that it is restricted by the mutawatir hadiths, for the hadiths indicating that cutting is not done for every theft, but rather has a nisab, have reached the level of tawatur, even if the specific hadiths specifying ten or less are ahad. In short: This is not a restriction of cutting by zanni, nor abrogation of the Qur'anic absolute by individual reports, but rather a restriction of cutting by qat'i, for the common element of the hadiths indicating restriction is mutawatir. Third: The reports specifying ten are contradicted by stronger reports: Malik, al-Bukhari, Muslim and others narrated from Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah cut off for a shield worth three dirhams, and al-Bukhari and Muslim also narrated as marfu': "The thief is not cut off except for a quarter of a dinar." There are many other reports in the Sunan and Musnads, and al-Shafi'i, Malik, and others argued with them. Its answer is from two aspects: First: When the hadiths conflicted on the amount for which a thief is cut off, we took the greater amount that is certain, and left the lesser that is not certain. Second: The reports specifying ten, although most are weak, created a doubt (shubha) regarding the obligation of cutting for less than ten, and it is established that punishments are warded off by doubts, as previously, so we ward off cutting from one who steals less than ten, and we require it for one who steals ten or more. By this, it becomes clear to you the refutation of what common people criticize the Hanafis for, that they abandoned in this chapter the practice of the hadiths of the Sahihayn, which are the most authentic books after the Book of Allah, and took weak reports. So preserve this, for it will benefit you in this world and the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is an expansion (sharh) on the nisab of theft from the matn of al-Wiqaya. The author begins by stating the chapter heading and then presents several hadiths that establish the nisab of ten dirhams, including a hadith from Ibn Abbas and a marfu' hadith from a man of Muzayna. These are the key evidences for the Hanafi position. Then the author anticipates three main objections against using these hadiths. The first objection is that the hadiths specifying ten dirhams are weak in chain. The response is that not all are weak, and the weakness is compensated by multiple paths (kathrat al-turuq). The second objection is that these are ahad hadiths, and restricting the absolute Qur'anic command (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:38) with ahad is against usul. The response is that the hadiths indicating the existence of a nisab are actually mutawatir, even if the specific number is from ahad; thus the restriction is by qat'i evidence, not zanni. The third objection is that the ten-dirham hadiths are contradicted by stronger hadiths from the Sahihayn mentioning three dirhams or a quarter dinar. The author gives a twofold response: first, when hadiths conflict on the amount, the greater amount (ten dirhams) is taken as the certain one, leaving the lesser as uncertain; second, even the weak hadiths create a doubt (shubha) about cutting for less than ten, and the principle of dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat (warding off punishments by doubts) applies. Thus the Hanafi ruling is that the hand is cut only for theft of ten dirhams or more. The passage concludes by defending the Hanafi school against common criticisms.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum value of stolen goods that triggers the hadd punishment of hand amputation.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or uncertainty that prevents the application of hudud punishments.
درء الحدود بالشبهات (dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat): The legal maxim that prescribed punishments are averted by doubts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum value (nisab) for which a thief's hand is cut off?
A) Three dirhams
B) A quarter dinar
C) Ten dirhams
D) Any amount

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi 10-dirham nisab for theft is derived by preferring the certain larger amount from conflicting hadiths and applying the maxim 'ward off hudud by doubts'.

Condition of Coining Dirhams for Theft Nisab
Commentary on two conditions for the nisab of theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Zahir al-Riwayah position on the condition of dirhams for the nisab of theft, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute from al-Hasan's narration, and apply the tarjih mechanism to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The nisab of theft requires coined dirhams in the authoritative view; a rare narration allows uncoined silver, but the relied-upon position is that 'dirham' customarily means minted coins.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies two conditions for the nisab of theft: the weight and the form of the dirhams. While the weight is settled, the requirement that the dirhams be coined (mudaraba) involves an intra-Hanafi dispute. We will examine the Zahir al-Riwayah position, the alternative narration, and the basis for preferring the requirement of coining.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
In Zahir al-Riwayah, dirhams must be coined; in Riwayat al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, coined and uncoined are the same.
2
Examine the basis for the first position
The hadith uses the term 'dirham', which customarily refers to coined silver.
Custom ('urf) is a recognized basis in Hanafi usul.
3
Recognize the authority weight
Zahir al-Riwayah is the authoritative transmission; the other is a nawadir (rare narration).
Nawadir are less relied upon unless necessary.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Marghinani and others consider the first position al-asahh (most correct) and it is the relied-upon fatwa.
Tarjih is based on strength of evidence and transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : دراهم ؛ والمعتبر في الدّراهم وزن سبعة مثاقيل على ما مر تحقيقه في «كتاب الزكاة»؛ لأنه المتعارف في عامة البلاد، واستقر عليه الأمر من عهد عمر كذا في «البناية».
قوله : مضروبة ؛ هذا في ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة : المضروب وغيره سواء. وجه الأوّل: وهو الأصح، كما في «الهداية»، وغيرها : إنّ حديث النّصاب ورد بلفظ الدرهم، واسمُ الدّرهم يطلق على المضروب عرفاً.
His saying: "Dirhams"; and the consideration in dirhams is the weight of seven mithqals, as its investigation passed in the Book of Zakat; because it is the customary usage in most lands, and the matter settled upon it from the time of Umar, as stated in al-Binaya. His saying: "Coined"; this is in Zahir al-Riwaya, and in the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: the coined and the uncoined are the same. The basis of the first, and it is the most correct, as in al-Hidaya and others: the hadith of the nisab came with the word dirham, and the name dirham is customarily applied to coined silver.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This is a short passage from the commentary explaining two key terms from the matn regarding the nisab of theft. First, it clarifies that the dirhams considered (for the nisab) are those weighing seven mithqals (approx. 3.36g?), as established in the chapter on zakat and based on the standard from Umar's time, according to al-Binaya. Second, it discusses the condition that the dirhams must be coined (mudaraba). The author states that in the Zahir al-Riwaya (the well-known and authoritative transmission), the dirhams must be minted coins. However, in a narration (riwaya) from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, both coined and uncoined silver are treated equally. The author then gives the basis for the first opinion (the need for coined dirhams), which is considered the most correct (al-asahh) according to al-Hidaya and others: the hadith on the nisab uses the word "dirham," and the name "dirham" is customarily applied specifically to coined silver. Therefore, for the hadd punishment, the stolen property must consist of coined silver dirhams.

Key Terms
دراهم مضروبة (darahim madrubah): Coined silver dirhams (minted coins) as opposed to uncoined silver or silver bullion.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known and authoritative transmission reported by Imam Muhammad's six core books.
رواية الحسن (Riwayat al-Hasan): A narration transmitted by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, considered a rare (nawadir) transmission.

The Nisab for Amputation: Hanafi Response to Criticism
Hanafi response to inter-madhhab dispute on nisab of theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning for setting the nisab of amputation at ten dirhams and defend it against the criticism of ignoring authentic hadiths.

Core Idea
When hadiths conflict on hadd amounts, Hanafis take the greater amount for certainty and use shubha to block punishment below it.

In this passage, the author defends the Hanafi position that amputation for theft requires a minimum of ten dirhams. Critics argue that authentic hadiths set a lower threshold, but the Hanafi response is twofold: choosing the larger amount out of caution and applying the principle that punishments are averted by any valid doubt. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how it refutes common objections.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: amputation is wajib only when the stolen property reaches ten dirhams or its equivalent.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opposition's evidence
Recognize that Shafi'i and Maliki schools cite authentic hadiths: the shield worth three dirhams and the quarter dinar threshold.
These are detailed in the source passage.
3
Follow the first Hanafi response
When hadiths conflict, Hanafis adopt the larger amount (ten dirhams) because it is certain, and they leave the lesser amount (three dirhams) as uncertain.
Based on the principle of taking the more cautious figure.
4
Follow the second Hanafi response
Even weak hadiths that specify ten dirhams create a shubha (doubt) that prevents amputation for less than ten, because punishments are repelled by doubts.
The maxim: dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf and rebuttal
Practical difference: Hanafis do not cut for less than ten dirhams; others cut at lower amounts. The criticism that Hanafis ignore authentic hadiths is thus refuted by showing they use a valid method.
The author concludes by saying this knowledge benefits in this world and the next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنّ أخبار التقدير بالعشرة معارضة بما هو أصح منها، فقد أخرج مالك والبخاري ومسلم وغيرهم، عن ابن عمر : إنّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قطع في ثمن مجنّ قيمته ثلاثة دراهم ، وأخرج البخاري ومسلم أيضاً مرفوعاً: «لا يقطع السارق إلا في ربع دينار) ، وفي الباب أخبار كثيرة مخرجة في السنن والمسانيد ، وبها احتج الشافعي ومالك وغيرهما.
وجوابه : من وجهين :
أحدهما : إنّ الأحاديث لما تعارضت في مقدار ما يقطع فيه السارق ، أخذنا بالأكثر المتيقن ، وتركنا الأقل الغير المتيقن.
وثانيهما : إن أخبار التقدير بالعشرة وإن كان أكثرها ضعيفاً أورثت شبهة في وجوب القطع في أقل من عشرة، وقد ثبت درء الحدود بالشبهات على ما مر فدرءنا الحد عمّن سرق أقل من العشرة ، وأوجبناه على من سرقها أو ما فوقها.
وبهذا ظهر لك دفع ما طعن به العوام على الحنفية من أنهم تركوا في هذا الباب العمل بأحاديث الصحيحين، وهمـا أصـح الكتب بعد كتاب الله، وأخذوا بأخبار ضعيفة، فاحفظ هذا فإنّه ينفعك في الدينا وفي الآخرة.
Third: The reports specifying (the nisab) as ten are opposed by what is more authentic than them. Malik, Bukhari, Muslim and others reported from Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) cut off (the hand) for a shield worth three dirhams. Bukhari and Muslim also reported marfu'an: 'The thief is not to be cut off except for a quarter dinar or more.' There are many other reports in the Sunan and Musnads, and al-Shafi'i, Malik, and others argued by them. The response is from two angles: First: When the hadiths are conflicting about the amount for which a thief is cut, we take the greater amount which is certain, and we leave the lesser amount which is uncertain. Second: Although most of the reports specifying ten (dirhams) are weak, they create a doubt (shubha) regarding the obligation of amputation for less than ten. It has been established that fixed punishments are repelled by doubts as previously mentioned, so we repel the amputation from one who steals less than ten, and we oblige it upon one who steals ten or more. By this it becomes clear to you the refutation of the criticism by the common people against the Hanafis that they abandoned in this topic the practice of the hadiths of the two Sahihs—which are the most authentic books after the Book of Allah—and took weak reports. So remember this, for it will benefit you in this world and the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The author addresses a common criticism that Hanafis ignore authentic hadiths on the nisab of theft. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools cite hadiths indicating a lower nisab: a shield worth three dirhams (three dirhams) and a quarter dinar. The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) When hadiths conflict on the amount, we take the larger amount (10 dirhams) because it is certain, and the smaller amount is uncertain. (2) Even if the hadiths specifying 10 dirhams are weak, they create a shubha (doubt) that prevents the application of the hadd punishment for amounts less than ten, because punishments are averted by doubts (dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat). Thus, the Hanafi position of requiring ten dirhams is based on precaution and legal principle, not disregard of authentic hadiths. The author concludes that this refutes the lay criticism.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity in the evidence or circumstances that prevents application of hadd punishment
درء الحدود بالشبهات (dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat): The legal maxim that prescribed punishments are to be averted by any valid doubt
نصاب (nisab): The minimum value of stolen property required for amputation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They reject those hadiths as fabricated
B) They consider the hadiths of ten dirhams more authentic
C) They take the larger amount due to uncertainty and apply the principle of repelling punishments by doubts
D) They follow a different textual source that explicitly states ten dirhams

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi nisab of ten dirhams is derived from taking the greater amount when evidence conflicts and from repelling punishments by doubt, not from disregarding authentic hadiths.

Condition that Dirhams Must Be Minted
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether nisab requires minted dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on minted dirhams for the nisab, distinguish between the Zahir al-Riwayah and the narration of Hasan, and apply the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
The Hanafi nisab for theft requires minted dirhams because 'dirham' customarily means minted coin.

In the Hanafi school, the nisab for theft is a key condition for applying the hadd. But what exactly counts as dirhams? Must they be minted coins or does any silver suffice? This lesson unpacks an internal Hanafi dispute on that very question, showing how custom and authority determine the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State clearly: the Zahir al-Riwayah requires minted dirhams (مضروبة); the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifah says minted and unminted are equal.
2
Find the evidence for each
For the Zahir al-Riwayah: the hadith uses the word 'dirham', which customarily refers to minted coins. For the alternate view: the term might include unminted silver of equivalent weight.
The passage only gives evidence for the first view.
3
Evaluate the tarjih
The passage explicitly says the first view is الأصح (the sounder) as per Al-Hidayah and others. Understand why: custom ('urf) determines the meaning, and the practice from Umar's time settled on minted dirhams.
Tarjih is based on custom and established practice.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that dirhams must be minted. The other narration is not acted upon in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : دراهم ; والمعتبر في الدّراهم وزن سبعة مثاقيل على ما مر تحقيقه في «کتاب الزكاة» ; لأنه المتعارف في عامة البلاد، واستقر عليه الأمر من عهد عمر كذا في «البناية».
[٢] قوله : مضروبة ; هذا في ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة : المضروب وغيره سواء. وجه الأوّل: وهو الأصح، كما في «الهداية»(۳)، وغيرها : إنّ حديث النّصاب ورد بلفظ الدرهم، واسمُ الدّرهم يطلق على المضروب عرفاً.
His statement: 'dirhams' — and the standard for dirhams is the weight of seven mithqals, as its investigation passed in the Book of Zakat, because it is the customary practice in most lands and the matter has been settled from the time of Umar, as in al-Binayah. [2] His statement: 'minted' — this is in the Zahir al-Riwayah. In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifah: minted and unminted are the same. The first view is the sounder, as in al-Hidayah and others, because the hadith on the nisab came with the word 'dirham', and the name 'dirham' is customarily applied to minted coins.

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the dirhams constituting the nisab must be minted (مضروبة) or if unminted silver also qualifies. The Zahir al-Riwayah (the established school position) requires minted dirhams. However, in a narration by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifah, minted and unminted are equal. The author notes that the first view is the sounder (asahh), because the hadith mentions 'dirham' and customarily this term refers to minted coins. Additionally, the weight standard for a dirham is seven mithqals, as settled from the time of Umar (may Allah be pleased with him).

Key Terms
مضروبة (madruba): Minted, stamped coins; the condition for the nisab in theft according to the relied-upon view
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani containing the primary transmitted rulings of the school

Conditions for Amputation in Theft (Hadd al-Sariqa)
Ruling on conditions for amputation and exception for mahram's house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions for amputation and explain why stealing from a mahram's house repels the hadd.

Core Idea
Amputation requires a perfect crime: mukallaf, nisab, hirz, no shubha.

The hadd punishment for theft is amputation, but it only applies when all conditions are met. The Hanafi school emphasizes the absence of any doubt (shubha) that could avert the punishment. This lesson breaks down each condition and the critical exception for property taken from a close relative's house.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Thief's Capacity
The text says 'mukallaf' meaning legally responsible. Note that children and insane are excluded because they lack criminal intent.
Mukallaf includes both free and slave.
2
Recognize the Hirz Requirement
The theft must be from a protected place (hirz) – a location where property is secured according to custom.
Custom determines what counts as a hirz.
3
Understand Shubha Exception
If the hirz itself carries a shubha, like a mahram's house where entry is permitted, the hadd is averted even if the stolen property belongs to someone else.
The permission to enter creates doubt in the security of the place.
4
Note the Nisab and Quality
The stolen property must be worth at least 10 dirhams (nisab). If the item is unminted gold worth less, or of poor quality, amputation is not required.
Quality (jā'id) was also stipulated for dirhams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكمها : القطع. فإن سرق مكلف حر أو عبد قدر النصاب محرزاً بلا شبهة، احتراز عما يكون في الحرز شبهة ، كما إذا سرق من بيت ذي رحم - ويؤيده أن شروط العقوباتِ تراعى على صفة الكمال رعاية لكمال الجناية، فلو سرق عشرة تبراً قيمتها أنقص من عشرة مضروبة، لا يجب القطع ; ولهذا شرطوا في الدراهم الجودة كما مرّ.
[١] قوله : مكلّف; احترز به عن الصبي والمجنون، فإنّ العقوبة لا تقام عليهما لعدم الجناية منهما.
[٢]قوله : حر أو عبد ; قال في «الهداية»: «العبد» والحر في القطع سواء ; لأنّ النّصّ لم يفصل، ولأن التنصيف متعذر فيتكامل ; صيانة لأموال الناس (۳).
[٣] قوله : كما إذا سرق من بيت ذي رحم محرم ; سواء سرق مال ذي رحم محرم أو مال غيره من بيته فلا قطع فيه ; لوجودِ الشَّبهةِ الدّارئة للحد في الحرز ; لوجود الإذنِ بالدخول في بيته من ذي رحم محرم منه، لا سيما إذا كانت بينهما قرابة الولاد.
Its ruling: amputation. So if a mukallaf, free or slave, steals the amount of nisab from a protected place without any shubha—an exclusion for what involves shubha in the hirz, like if he steals from the house of a relative (dhi rahm). This is supported by the fact that conditions of punishments are observed in a perfect manner out of consideration for the completeness of the crime. If someone steals ten pieces of gold (tibr) whose value is less than ten minted dirhams, amputation is not obligatory; for this reason they stipulated quality in dirhams as previously mentioned. [1] His statement: 'mukallaf' excludes the child and the insane, for punishment is not established against them due to absence of crime. [2] His statement: 'free or slave' — al-Hidayah says: the slave and free are equal in amputation because the text did not differentiate, and because half is not possible so it is completed for the protection of people's wealth. [3] His statement: 'as if he steals from the house of a close blood relative (dhi rahm mahram)' — whether he steals the property of that relative or the property of someone else from that house, there is no amputation due to the existence of a shubha that repels the hadd in the hirz, because there is permission to enter the house from that close relative, especially when there is a blood relationship.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining the conditions for amputation. The thief must be mukallaf (legally responsible), excluding children and the insane. Both free and slave are subject to amputation equally because the texts do not discriminate and splitting the punishment is impossible. The theft must be from a protected place (hirz) without any shubha. If there is shubha in the hirz, like stealing from the house of a close blood relative (mahram), then no amputation applies because the relative's permission to enter creates a doubt that repels the hadd. Additionally, if the stolen item is gold (tibr) whose value is less than ten minted dirhams, amputation is not required; quality is also a condition.

Key Terms
مكلف (mukallaf): A person who is legally responsible: sane, adult, and having reached puberty
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is kept protected
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that averts the hadd punishment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the relative's property is not considered protected
B) Because the thief has permission to enter, creating a shubha that repels the hadd
C) Because the nisab is lower in such cases
D) Because the relative must testify first

Key Takeaway
Amputation requires a perfect crime: the thief must be legally responsible, the property must reach nisab, theft must be from a protected place, and no doubt (shubha) must exist, such as stealing from a mahram's house.

The Six Questions in Theft Testimony
Explains six questions judge must ask theft witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six questions a Hanafi judge must ask theft witnesses and explain the legal purpose of each.

Core Idea
Six questions safeguard the hadd — without verifying how, what, when, where, how much, and from whom, the hand cannot be cut.

This passage from al-Wiqaya and its commentary details the procedural obligations of a judge when witnesses testify to theft. The judge must ask six specific questions to ensure all conditions for the hadd penalty are met. These questions are not mere formalities; they are essential to distinguish theft from other acts and to verify jurisdiction, nisab, and the absence of mahram relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six questions
List each of the six questions mentioned in the matn: How was it? What was it? When? Where? How much? From whom? Note the Arabic phrases.
The Arabic terms: كيف هي, ما هي, متى, أين هي, كم هي, ممن سرق.
2
Understand the purpose of each question
For each question, explain what condition it verifies: 'How' distinguishes theft from other acts; 'What' confirms it is a theft; 'When' checks for staleness; 'Where' ensures Dar al-Islam; 'How much' verifies nisab; 'From whom' excludes mahram.
The commentary elaborates on 'what' to exclude eavesdropping, deficient prayer, plagiarism, and highway robbery.
3
Note the procedural details from commentary
The commentary adds that only two male witnesses suffice, women's testimony is only accepted for property not for cutting, and the confessor is not asked about time.
This is important for understanding who is asked what.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a testimony, determine if the hadd can be applied based on the witnesses' answers to the six questions.
If any question is not satisfactorily answered, the hand is not cut.
5
Recognize the wisdom behind the questions
These questions prevent wrongful amputation by ensuring all conditions are met, reflecting the Hanafi emphasis on caution in hudud.
Hudud are suspended by doubts (shubuhat).

Source Text

Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqaya with commentary from Jamal al-Din al-Qunawi. It states that when two male witnesses testify for theft, the judge must ask them six specific questions: How was the theft? (i.e., was it done secretly or openly?), What was stolen? (to distinguish theft from other acts like eavesdropping, deficient prayer, plagiarism, or highway robbery), When did it occur? (to check if too much time has passed, which prevents the hadd), Where? (to ensure it was in Dar al-Islam, as theft in Dar al-Harb does not incur cutting), How much? (to confirm it reached the nisab threshold), and From whom? (to rule out theft from a mahram relative, which does not incur cutting). The commentary explains that only two male witnesses are needed, unlike zina which requires four. Women's testimony is not accepted for establishing the hadd of theft but is accepted for the financial aspect. The issue of secrecy (khafiyya) is clarified: in regular theft, secrecy from the owner is required, but in highway robbery (sariqa kubra), secrecy is not a condition. The judge's duty to ask these questions is a procedural obligation (wajib) to ensure all conditions for the hadd are met.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahada): Testimony of witnesses in a legal proceeding; here specifically regarding theft.
خفية (khafiyya): Secrecy; the condition that the theft must be done secretly from the owner.
نصاب (nisab): Minimum value of stolen goods required for the hadd of cutting.

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Joint Theft: Punishment of Participants in Hanafi Fiqh
Determines punishment when multiple persons participate in theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on punishment for participants in a joint theft and identify the condition that determines who receives the hadd of cutting.

Core Idea
Only the one who actually removes the stolen property from the safe place (hirz) is cut; others receive ta'zir and imprisonment.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the Hanafi position on joint theft. It explains that the severe punishment of hand-cutting (hadd) is specifically for the person who physically removes the property from its secured place. Accomplices who merely enter the premises or assist in other ways are not subject to the hadd but receive discretionary punishment (ta'zir). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the hudud penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key action
Determine who in the group performed the act of removing the stolen property from the hirz (safe place).
The hadd is tied to this specific act.
2
Distinguish participants
Separate the one who removed (mukhrij) from those who only entered or assisted.
Only the mukhrij may be subject to hadd.
3
Apply the ruling
If the remover is known, he alone is cut; if unknown, all receive ta'zir.
Imprisonment lasts until repentance.
4
Note the consequence
Accomplices are given ta'zir and imprisoned until they repent.
This is a discretionary punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فإن شارك جمع فيها، وأصاب كلاً. فإن شارك جمع فيها، وأصاب كلاً. المراد بالجمع ما فوق الواحد، فإن الحكم في الاثنين كذلك. والمراد بالمشاركة المشاركة في الدخول في الحرز بقرينة قوله: وإن أخذ بعضهم بالواو الوصلية أي وإن أخذ المال المسروق، وأخرجه من الحرز بعضهم ولم يشتركوا فيه. فلو دخل بعضهم واشتركوا بعد ذلك في فعل السرقة لا يقطع إلا الداخل إن عرف بعينه، وإن لم يعرف عزروا كلهم، وأبد حبسهم إلى أن تظهر توبتهم. كذا في «الفتح».
His saying: 'If a group participates in it, and it affects each...' The meaning of 'group' is more than one, for the ruling regarding two is likewise. And the meaning of participation is participation in entering the safe place, as indicated by his saying: 'And if some of them take it' with the conjunctive waw, i.e., if some of them take the stolen property and remove it from the safe place while others do not share in that. If some enter and they later participate in the act of theft, only the one who entered is cut if he is known; if not known, all are disciplined (ta'zir) and imprisoned until their repentance becomes evident. As in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a group of people jointly commits theft. The commentary clarifies that 'group' (jama') means more than one, and the same ruling applies to two persons. The key condition for cutting is actual removal of the stolen item from the safe place (hirz). If some participants only entered the safe place but did not physically remove the property, they are not subject to the hadd. If it is known which individual removed the property, that person alone is cut; the others receive ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and are imprisoned until they repent. If the actual remover is unknown, all are given ta'zir. This is based on the principle that the hadd of theft is only incurred by the one who directly effects the removal (akhraj) from the hirz. The proof is analogical: the hadd is specifically for the thief who takes the property, not for accomplices who merely assist without removing. This ruling is from the Hanafi school as recorded in Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd.
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is kept; removal from here is a condition for the hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Had of cutting
B) Ta'zir and imprisonment until repentance
C) No punishment
D) Same as the remover

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd of theft is applied only to the person who physically removes the property from the secure place; all other participants receive ta'zir and imprisonment.

The Questions in a Theft Case and the Ruling on Multiple Thieves
Commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah about theft procedure and multiple thieves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four preliminary questions asked in a theft case and apply the Hanafi rule that by istihsan all participants who entered the hirz are liable for hadd amputation, even if only one carries the item, provided each obtains the nisab.

Core Idea
Theft begins with entering the hirz; by istihsan all participants share the hadd, not just the carrier.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the legal investigation a judge must conduct in a theft case: he asks about the antiquity of the theft, its location (Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb), the quantity of the stolen item (to check nisab), and the identity of the thief (to exclude mahram relatives). It then addresses a key intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute: when multiple thieves cooperate but only one physically removes the property from the secure place, should all be subject to amputation? The Hanafi school, by istihsan, amputates all who entered the hirz, while other schools and Zufar restrict amputation to the carrier by qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four preliminary questions
The matn mentions four questions about the theft: (1) Is it old (taqadum)? (2) Did it occur in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb? (3) What is the value of the stolen item (does it reach nisab)? (4) Who is the thief (is he a mahram relative?). Each question determines whether the hadd punishment applies.
Antiquity prevents hadd, not financial liability. Location in Dar al-Harb prevents hadd. Nisab (10 dirhams) is required. Mahram relatives are not amputated.
2
Understand the effect of taqadum
If the theft occurred long ago, the judge does not apply the hadd amputation, but the thief must still return the stolen property. This is a defense specific to the penal aspect, not the financial liability.
An objection is raised about evidence vs confession, but the answer is that da'wa (claim) is a condition for money, not for cutting.
3
Analyze the multiple thieves scenario
When a group participates in the theft, the key factor is whether each entered the hirz. If each obtains the nisab share, all are amputated. If some entered but others did not, only those who entered are amputated (if known). If the carrier is unknown, all are disciplined and imprisoned.
The text clarifies that 'participation' means entering the hirz together, as indicated by the phrase 'and if some took it'.
4
Contrast qiyas and istihsan
By strict analogy (qiyas) to divisible acts like property taking, only the person who physically carries the item out of the hirz should be amputated. This is the view of Zufar and the three other Sunni imams. The Hanafi school adopts istihsan: because the act of theft begins with entering the hirz, a collective step found from all, all are amputated to close the door to corruption.
The response to the qiyas argument from qisas (retaliation) is that qisas involves an indivisible act (taking life), while theft is divisible, but istihsan overrides due to the shared inception.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under Hanafi law, any person who enters the hirz with the intent to steal and whose share meets nisab is liable for amputation, even if another carries the goods. Under Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali, and Zufar's view, only the carrier is amputated.
This difference affects prosecution strategies and the number of potential amputations in gang theft cases.

If a group participated in it (the theft) and each got (a share)... so that it may be known whether it (the theft) is old or not. And about where it was? i.e. in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb. And how much it is? (This) refers to the stolen item, and the intended meaning is the stolen item. He asks about the quantity so that it may be known whether the stolen item reached the nisab or not. And who stole? So that it may be known whether he is from a mahram relative or not. If a group participated in it and each got (a share)... [1] His saying: 'so that it may be known whether it is old or not' – so he is not flogged if the theft is old. An objection is raised against this: the witness in delaying the testimony here is not accused, because it is not accepted without a claim. So it should not be asked when proven by evidence, just as it is not asked when proven by confession, because antiquity does not prevent the validity of confession. And it is answered: the claim is a condition for financial liability but not for amputation, so antiquity prevents amputation but not financial liability. This is in al-Nihaya. [2) His saying: 'i.e. in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb' – if it is known that it was in Dar al-Harb, then no amputation. [3] His saying: 'And the intended meaning is the stolen item' – because 'al-sariqa' may be used to mean the stolen item, like 'al-khalq' meaning the created thing. He needed this intended meaning because 'al-sariqa' in its infinitive sense is an act that is not described by quantity. [4] His saying: 'If a group participated in it' – the meaning of 'group' is more than one, for the ruling applies to two as well. The meaning of participation is participation in entering the hira, supported by his saying: 'and if some took it', with the conjunctive waw, i.e. if some took the stolen money and removed it from the hira without the others participating in it. So if some entered and then participated in the act of theft, only the one who entered is amputated if he is known specifically, and if not known, all are disciplined and imprisoned until their repentance becomes apparent. This is in al-Fath. (1) Fath al-Qadir (5:390). Book of theft: its pillar, its locus, its nisab, and its ruling. (They are) amputated if (each got) the amount of nisab, and if some took (them). [1] His saying: 'i.e. each one' – he indicates that the tanwin in the matn is in place of the annexed word. [2] His saying: 'the amount of nisab' – he said in al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: 'If he got less, he is not amputated, but he guarantees what he got of that.' End. An objection is raised against this: if a group kills one, all are killed even if the act of killing is not found from each of them, so similarly in theft by participation, each should be amputated even if the full nisab is not obtained from each. And it is answered: retaliation is connected to the removal of life, which is indivisible, so it is attributed to each of them completely, unlike theft. (3) His saying: 'they are amputated' – this is istihsan, and by qiyas only the carrier is amputated, and Zufar and the three Imams said this. The face of istihsan is that theft begins with entering the hira, and this was found from all of them, so amputation of all is required to close the door of corruption, as in al-Fath (3). (1) That is, whether they left with him or after him immediately, or he left after them immediately. See: Fath Bab al-Inaya (3:241). (2) From al-Jawhara al-Nayyira (2:165). (3) Fath al-Qadir (5:389-390).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the questions to be asked in a theft case and the ruling when multiple thieves participate. The commentator explains each phrase of the matn. First, the matn mentions that the thief is asked about the antiquity of the theft to see if it is old; the commentary clarifies that if the theft is old, the hadd punishment is not applied, but financial liability remains. An objection is raised that if the theft is proven by evidence (bayyinah) or confession (iqrar), antiquity should not matter, but the answer is that the claim (da'wa) is a condition for financial liability, not for the hadd amputation, so antiquity prevents only the hadd. Second, the matn asks about the location to see if the theft occurred in Dar al-Harb; if so, no amputation. Third, the matn asks about the quantity; the commentary clarifies that 'al-sariqa' here means the stolen item itself, not the act of stealing, because the act cannot be described by quantity. Fourth, the matn asks about the identity of the thief to see if he is a mahram relative. Then the matn discusses multiple thieves: if a group participates in entering the hira (secure place) and each gets a share equal to nisab, they are all amputated. However, if some entered and others did not, only those who entered are amputated. The commentary then addresses the case where only some take the item: by qiyas, only the carrier should be amputated, as argued by Zufar and the three other imams, but by istihsan (juristic preference), all participants are amputated because the act of theft begins with entering the hira, and all shared in that. The Hanafi view is that this istihsan is necessary to close the door to corruption. The text also includes an analogy from qisas (retaliation) to argue that amputation should apply to all, but the answer distinguishes because qisas deals with an indivisible act (taking life), while theft is divisible.

Key Terms
تقادم (taqadum): The antiquity or oldness of the theft; if the theft occurred long ago, the hadd punishment is not applied but financial liability remains.
حرز (hirz): A secure place of storage for property; entering the hirz is the beginning of the theft act for the purpose of the hadd.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum value (10 dirhams or equivalent) of the stolen item required for the application of the hadd amputation.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference overriding qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling; here, it requires amputation of all participants despite the divisibility of the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling when a group of thieves enters a secure place (hirz) and only one of them removes the stolen item, but each member's share of the item is less than the nisab?
A) All are amputated because they participated in entering the hirz.
B) Only the one who carried the item out is amputated.
C) None are amputated because no one obtained the full nisab.
D) All are imprisoned but not amputated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab uses istihsan to amputate all thieves who entered the hirz, emphasizing the collective nature of the crime's inception, but each must have individually obtained the nisab; otherwise, only financial liability applies.

Ruling on Joint Theft When Only One Takes the Nisab
Explains the Hanafi ruling on joint theft with istihsan proof and refutation of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi istihsan ruling to a case of joint theft and distinguish it from the qiyas-based ruling using the concept of divisibility.

Core Idea
In joint theft, all participants face amputation because theft begins with entering the secure place, even if only one carries the item out.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya confronts a critical question: when multiple thieves cooperate but only one physically removes the stolen goods, should all be amputated or only the carrier? The Hanafi school answers through istihsan (juristic preference), departing from strict analogy (qiyas). By studying this lesson, you will understand how the Hanafi jurists prioritize deterrence and the shared commencement of the crime over the mechanical act of taking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
The base text states: if they (participants) took a nisab (minimum threshold), they are all amputated, even if only some took it. The condition: total stolen must reach nisab.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
The objector argues by analogy to qisas (retaliation): if a group kills one, all are killed, so in joint theft all should be amputated even if one didn't take a full nisab.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the qiyas objection
The Hanafi response refutes the analogy: qisas involves removing the soul, which is indivisible, so each killer is fully culpable. Theft, however, is divisible – the act of taking can be split among participants. Therefore, the qiyas fails.
4
Examine the istihsan basis
The text states that the correct ruling (all amputated) is based on istihsan. The reasoning: theft begins with entering the secure place (hirz), and all participants engaged in that entry. Thus, all are liable. This is to close the door of corruption (sadd al-bab al-fasad).
5
Contrast with the opposing view
Zufar and the three other Imams hold that only the carrier should be amputated based on qiyas (the act of taking is the operative act). The Hanafi istihsan is the mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) because it better serves justice and deterrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة ركنها ومحلها ونصابها وحكمها قدر نصاب قطعوا، وإن أخذ بعضُهم أي كل واحد، قدر نصاب) قطعوا ، وإن أخذ بعضُهم) (١) : أي مع أنَّ الأخذ صدر من بعضهم فقط. [١]قوله : أي كل واحد ؛ أشار به إلى أن التنوين في المتن عوض عن المضاف إليه. [٢] قوله : قدر نصاب ؛ قال في «الجوهرة النيرة» : «فلو أصابه أقل لم يقطع ، بل يضمن ما أصابه من ذلك». انتهى) وأورد عليه : بأنه إذا قتل جمع واحد يقتل كلهم وإن لم يوجد من كل منهم القتل " كلاً ، فكذلك ينبغي في السرقة بالتشارك أن يقطع كل منهم ، وإن لم يصب منهم قدر النصاب. كاملاً وأجيب عنه : بأن القصاص متعلق بإخراج الروح، وهو غير متجزئ، فيضافُ إلى كل منهم كاملاً بخلاف السرقة. (٣) قوله : قطعوا ؛ هذا استحسان، والقياس أن يقطع الحامل وحده، وبه قال زفر والأئمة الثلاثة، وجه الاستحسان : إنّ السرقة مبدؤها الدخول في الحرز، وقد وُجِدَ من الجميع فوجب قطع الكلِّ سداً لباب الفساد، كذا في «الفتح»(٣).
Book of Theft: its pillar, its subject, its nisab, and its ruling. 'They took a nisab, they are amputated, even if some of them took it.' That is, each one. (They took a nisab) they are amputated, even if some of them took it: that is, even though the taking occurred only from some of them. [1] He said: 'that is, each one'; he indicated by this that the nunation in the matn substitutes for the genitive addition. [2] He said: 'the amount of nisab'; he said in al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: 'If he took less than that, he is not amputated, but he guarantees what he took from that.' End. And it was objected: when a group kills one person, all of them are killed even if the killing from each one of them was not complete, so likewise in theft by participation, every one of them should be amputated even if the amount of nisab was not taken by them completely. And it was answered: Retaliation pertains to extracting the soul, which is indivisible, so it is attributed to each of them completely, unlike theft. (3) He said: 'they are amputated'; this is istihsan, and qiyas would be that only the carrier is amputated, and Zufar and the three Imams said this. The basis of istihsan: the beginning of theft is entering the hirz, and that occurred from all of them, so amputation of all is obligatory as a way to close the door of corruption, as stated in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya explains the Hanafi ruling when multiple people cooperate in theft but only one physically takes the item. The base text states: 'If they took a nisab amount, they are amputated, even if only some of them took it.' The commentator clarifies that 'they' refers to each participant, so each one is liable for amputation provided the total stolen reaches the nisab threshold. An objection is raised using the analogy of qisas (retaliation): if a group kills one person, all are executed even if each individual's contribution was insufficient to cause death. Why not apply the same logic to theft? The Hanafi response distinguishes: qisas involves removing the soul (ruh), which is non-divisible, so the full act is attributed to each killer. Theft, however, is divisible—the act of taking can be split. Therefore, the qiyas analogy fails. The commentator then states that the ruling 'they are amputated' is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The qiyas would dictate only the one who physically carries the item out of the hirz is amputated, as held by Zufar and the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali). The Hanafi istihsan is justified because the crime of theft begins with entering the secure place (hirz), and all participants engaged in that entry. To prevent rampant theft and corruption, all must be subjected to the hadd penalty. This ruling is found in al-Fath as well. Thus, the Hanafi position prioritizes deterrence over strict analogy.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqa): The act of taking another's wealth secretly from its secure place (hirz) with the intent to steal.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum value threshold triggering amputation, commonly set at 10 dirhams or a quarter dinar.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from strict qiyas in favor of a ruling that better serves justice or public interest.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction; applying the ruling of an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause.
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is stored; theft must involve removal from hirz for amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for amputating all participants in a joint theft when only one takes the nisab?
A) Strict analogy (qiyas) with individual theft
B) Juristic preference (istihsan) considering the shared entry into the secure place
C) Consensus of the four schools
D) The indivisibility of the stolen property

Key Takeaway
In joint theft, the Hanafi school amputates all participants because the crime begins with entering the secure place together, even if only one physically removes the item.

Valuable Materials for Hadd: The Hanafi List and Conditions
Lists materials considered valuable for hadd amputation in theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the valuable materials that trigger hadd amputation for theft according to the Hanafi school and identify the conditions (muhraz, non-mubah, movable door) that must be met.

Core Idea
Hadd applies only to stolen items that are both valuable and secured, excluding materials that are originally permissible (mubah).

This passage from the chapter on hadd for theft (sariqa) specifies the materials for which the hand of the thief is amputated. The Hanafi school requires that the stolen item be a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that is secured (muhraz) and not originally ownerless (mubah al-asl). In this lesson, we will examine the list of materials and the subtle conditions that determine when the punishment is applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed materials
Read the passage and identify each material mentioned: teakwood, cane, ebony, sandalwood, green bezels, rubies, emeralds, pearls, and a wooden vessel or door. Note that these are rare, valuable, and not found in nature as ownerless.
These materials are considered like gold and silver in value.
2
Understand the condition of being 'muhraz' (secured)
The commentary clarifies that the door must not be fixed in the wall; if it is fixed and uprooted, it is not considered secured (muhraz), so no hadd. This teaches that the stolen item must be taken from a secure location.
Even if a fixed door secures the house, itself is not muhraz.
3
Apply the exclusion for 'mubah al-asl' items
Items that are originally permissible without ownership (like wild wood or stones) are excluded. If the vessel or door is made of a material that is not mubah (e.g., metal), cutting applies by greater reason.
Mubah al-asl items are not considered mal mutaqawwim.
4
Derive the general principle
Synthesize: Hadd for theft requires that the stolen item be valuable (like the listed materials), secured (muhraz), and not mubah al-asl. The door example shows an additional nuance: physical fixation removes the 'secured' status.

Chapter of what the hand is cut off for and what it is not cut off for. And the hand is cut off for (theft of) teakwood, cane, ebony, sandalwood, green bezels, rubies, emeralds, pearls, and a vessel and a door made of wood. [1] His statement: 'for teakwood ... etc.': Teakwood is a well-known wood, it is the Arabicized form of 'sal'. Al-Zamakhshari said: It is a heavy black wood imported from India, and the earth hardly erodes it. And some said: Teak resembles ebony, but it is less black. Ebony: with a madd of the hamza and fatha of the ba and damm of the noon after waw, followed by sin muhmalah, and it is also said with damm of the ba: a well-known wood, its Arabic name is sasam (like ja'far). Qana: with fatha of qaf, a maqsura: plural of qanah, which is the wood of the spear. Sandalwood: on the pattern of ja'far, a tree with a pleasant scent, well-known. Fussus: with damm, plural of fass al-khatam: with fatha of fa and tashdid of the sad: in Persian: nagniye angushtari (setting of the ring). Colors. The restriction to 'green' (al-khudr) with damm, plural of akhdar, is accidental, for the ruling is unified for all. [2] His statement: 'and the door': what is meant is the door that is not fixed in the wall, rather it is secured (muhraz). But if it is fixed in the wall and he uproots it and takes it, then his hand is not cut off, because cutting is only for (theft of) secured property, not for unsecured property, even if it secures the premises, as will come. [3] His statement: 'made of' is a hal from door and vessel, and he indicated by it that if they were made of other than wood that does not exist as an originally permissible (mubah al-asl) item, then cutting is with greater reason. Stone. (1) Ebony: a tree like a piece on top of which grows a green plant, its wood is very hard, it does not float on water but sinks, and it resembles stone. See: 'Aja'ib al-Makhluqat (2:4). (2) Because they are among the most precious and valuable of properties and they do not exist as an originally permissible item in their form in the land of Islam that is not desired, so they become like gold and silver. See: al-Hidayah (2:121).

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on hadd for theft (sariqa) lists the specific materials for which the punishment of amputation of the hand is inflicted upon the thief. The Hanafi school holds that hadd applies only when the stolen item is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that is generally considered precious and is not readily available as ownerless (mubah). The commentary explains each term: teak, ebony, sandalwood, etc., emphasizing that these are rare and valuable. It also clarifies two key conditions: (1) The door must not be fixed in the wall; if it is fixed and uprooted, it is not considered secured (muhraz) and hadd does not apply. (2) The vessel and door must be made of wood; if made of other materials that are not originally permissible (like stone or metal that are not mubah), cutting applies with even greater reason. The footnotes reinforce that these items are like gold and silver in value and scarcity, which is why they are included.

Key Terms
محرز (muhraz): Property that is secured in a locked or guarded place; theft from such a place triggers hadd.
مباح الأصل (mubah al-asl): Items that are originally permissible to own without ownership, like wild wood or stones found in nature.
قطع (qat'): Amputation of the right hand as a hadd punishment for theft.

Valuable vs. Trifling Items in Theft Amputation
Defines valuable and trifling items for theft hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valuable items that incur hand amputation and trifling items that do not, applying the Hanafi criteria of manufactured value and permissible origin.

Core Idea
A stolen item triggers amputation only if it is valuable and not a freely available trifle.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lists specific costly goods like sandalwood, gems, and manufactured doors, then excludes natural items like wood, grass, and fish. The Hanafi school uses the concept of tāfih (trifling) to define when the hadd penalty applies. We will unpack the conditions for amputation and understand the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage lists items 'cut for' (valuable manufactured/rare items) and items 'not cut for' (trifling natural items). List each category from the text.
2
Analyze the definition of tāfih
A trifle is an item whose raw material exists as permissible origin in Dar al-Islam, has no manufactured value, and is not desired by people. Note the examples: wood, grass, reed, fish, game.
The concept of 'desirability' is key; sugarcane is desired so it is not a trifle.
3
Apply the manufactured-value rule
Even if an item's raw material is a trifle, once it is manufactured (e.g., a wooden box or door), it becomes valuable and amputation applies. The commentary explicitly states this.
The same logic applies to items made from non-wood materials that are not permissible origin.
4
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school does not have the tāfih exclusion; they require the item to be valuable (mutaqawwim) but do not exclude items freely found in nature per se.
This difference in usul leads to different outcomes in some theft cases.

Chapter on what is cut for and what is not. And the hand is cut for [wood of] saj, qana, abnus, sandal, green gemstones, ruby, beryl, pearl, and a vessel and a door if made from wood. He said: 'Not for a trifling thing that exists as permissible in our land, such as wood, grass, reed, fish, and game.' [Commentary:] His statement: 'by saj...' etc.; saj is a well-known wood, an arabicization of 'sal', al-Zamakhshari said it is heavy black wood imported from India, hardly decayed by earth; some said saj resembles abnus but less black. Abnus: known wood, Arabic name 'sasim'. Qana: plural of qanat, spear shaft. Sandal: size of Ja'far, a sweet-scented tree. Fuss: plural of fass (gem), Persian 'nangini angushtari'. Restriction to 'green' is coincidental; the ruling is uniform for all colors. His statement: 'door' means not fixed to the wall but stored; if fixed, pulling it does not cut because cutting is only for stored goods. His statement: 'made of' indicates if made of other than wood that is not permissible origin, cutting is more appropriate. His statement: 'not for a trifling thing...' rebuts a possible confusion: these items are of the type of wood and stone, which are permissible origin in Dar al-Islam, so one might think there is no cutting for them; hence they were listed. His statement: 'in deserts' plural of sahara'. His statement: 'not for a trifling thing' means one does not cut for stealing a trifling thing, which is what is found permissible in Dar al-Islam and has no manufactured value and is not desired; but after manufacture, cutting applies, like a vessel or door made of wood. Also cutting applies for desired things like gold, silver, gems, pearls. His statement: 'like wood' also includes potash, coal, salt, pottery. 'Reed' is for pens etc., except sugarcane which is desired, so cutting applies. 'Fish' fresh or salted. 'Game' is wild animal fleeing by nature, either by legs or wings, so fish is not included.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a ruling stating which stolen items incur the hadd penalty of hand amputation and which do not, with explanatory commentary. The chapter opens by listing valuable rare items: certain expensive woods (saj, qana, abnus, sandal), precious gemstones (ruby, beryl, pearl), and manufactured items like a door or vessel made of wood. The commentary clarifies each term: saj is a heavy black wood from India, abnus is known black wood, qana is spear shafts, sandal is sandalwood, fuss are gemstones. It notes that the mention of 'green' gemstones is incidental; the ruling applies to all colors. For the door and vessel, the condition 'made of wood' is emphasized; if made of other valuable materials not freely available, amputation applies even more. The second part states that no amputation occurs for trifling items whose raw material is freely found in Dar al-Islam, such as wood, grass, reed, fish, and game. The commentary explains that these were listed to prevent misunderstanding: since wood and stone are permissible origin, one might think that any item of that kind is exempt, but the ruling clarifies that it is only the unmanufactured, undesired items. After manufacture (e.g., a wooden door), the item becomes valuable and amputation applies. Also, items naturally desired like sugarcane are excluded from the exemption. The key principle is: the stolen item must be valuable (mutaqawwim) and not a trifle that exists freely in nature without manufacturing effort. The Hanafi school uses this to define the minimum value (nisab) and guard against applying the hadd for petty theft.

Key Terms
الساج (al-sāj): A known heavy black wood imported from India, arabicized from 'sal'
الآبنوس (al-ābnūs): A known wood, hard and black, Arabic name 'sasim'
التافه (al-tāfih): A trifling thing whose genus exists as permissible origin in Dar al-Islam without manufactured value and is not desired by people

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, for which of the following stolen items does hand amputation apply?
A) A piece of raw wood from the forest
B) A wooden door that was manufactured and stored
C) A fresh fish from the river
D) A wild bird caught from the desert

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft requires the item to be valuable (mutaqawwim) and not a trifle freely available in nature; manufactured items from such trifles become subject to the hadd.
