Definition of Tafih and Perishable Items in Theft Hadd
Defines items exempt from hadd due to triviality or quick spoilage with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi criteria for exempting items from the hadd of theft and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding tafih and perishable items.

Core Idea
A stolen item's value matters: trivial or quickly-spoiling items may spare the thief from amputation, but not every low-value item qualifies.

The hadd penalty for theft is severe, so the Hanafi school carefully defines the conditions that trigger it. One critical condition is that the stolen item must be valuable (mutaqawwim). This passage explores two categories of items that are not considered valuable for hadd purposes: tafih (trivial) items whose type exists as mubah originally, and items that spoil quickly. The text records a significant intra-Hanafi dispute, as Abu Yusuf holds a far narrower view on what counts as trivial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi majority position
Review the list of items exempt by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: tafih items (wood, grass, reed, fish, game) and perishable items (milk, meat, fresh fruit, dates on tree, watermelon) plus certain minerals.
2
Explore the rationale for exemption
Understand why these items are exempt: because their type is originally mubah (permissible in origin) or they spoil quickly, thus lacking full property value or stable guarding (hirz).
Rationale based on the concept of mutaqawwim value.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's counter-position
Note that Abu Yusuf exempts only mud, dirt, and dung. What might be his reasoning based on a strict interpretation of nisab or deterrent purpose?
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority position within the Hanafi school.
4
Analyze the practical difference
Consider a concrete scenario: theft of a piece of wood from a guarded market. Under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, no hadd because wood is tafih; under Abu Yusuf, hadd applies because wood is not mud or dung.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify which view is relied upon for fatwa: the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is more detailed and widely transmitted.
Later Hanafi authorities prefer the broader exemption.

[8] His statement: 'except mud and dirt' - the difference between them: mud is applied to dirt mixed with water and wet, its Persian: gil-e namnak, and dirt is more general than it, and what is meant here is what is contrasted with it by context. And 'dung' - with kasr of the sin and qaf, with a sukun ra between them - Arabized: sarkin.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses an important detail in the law of theft (sariqa): not every stolen item triggers the hadd penalty of amputation. The text defines two categories of exempt items: (1) tafih (trivial) items whose type exists as mubah (permissible) in the Abode of Islam, and (2) items that spoil quickly (ma yafsidu sari'an).

The commentary explains that the author counted these specific examples—wood, grass, reed, fish, and game—to counter a possible misunderstanding. Since these items are of the same type as wood and stone found in deserts and mountains (which are mubah in origin and thus no hadd for stealing them), one might wrongly assume that even when these items are owned and guarded, there is still no hadd. Thus the author clarifies that the exemption applies only when the item is still in its mubah original state; once a valuable craft is made (e.g., a wooden door), or if the item is intrinsically desirable (e.g., sugar cane, metals, jewels), then the hadd applies.

Then the text adds another category: items that spoil quickly, such as milk, meat, fresh fruit, dates on a tree, and watermelon. The rationale is that such items lack stability for guarding (hirz) or have no significant value due to rapid spoilage. Even after drying, if guarded, the hadd applies.

The passage then records an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the exemption applies to the listed items. However, Abu Yusuf holds a much narrower view: he does not cut the hand for anything except mud, dirt, and dung (i.e., these three are the only truly trivial items). So for Abu Yusuf, stealing any other item, even if of low value, triggers the hadd, provided the conditions of theft are met. This is a significant ikhtilaf that affects practice.

The commentary also defines key terms: 'tafih' is something whose type is mubah in origin, with no valuable craft, and not desired. 'Sa'id' refers to wild animals that flee by their nature (except fish). Zemikh, maghrah, and nurah are types of minerals/earth. The difference between teen (mud) and turab (dirt) is explained: mud is wet dirt, dirt is general.

In practice, the mufta bihi (relied-upon position) for the Hanafi school is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is more detailed and aligns with the general principle that the hadd is not applied for trivialities. However, the dispute is noted for academic rigor.

Key Terms
تافه (tafih): A stolen item that is considered trivial: its type exists as mubah in origin in the Abode of Islam, no valuable craft has been made in it, and it is not desired by people.
صيد (sayd): Wild game animals that are naturally untamed, whether by legs or wings; fish is excluded.
يفسد سريعاً (yafsidu sari'an): Items that spoil quickly within a short time, rendering them no longer beneficial (e.g., milk, meat, fresh fruit).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad exempt more items from hadd, while Abu Yusuf exempts only mud, dirt, and dung.
B) Abu Yusuf exempts more items, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad exempt only wood and stone.
C) They all agree on the same list of exempt items.
D) The dispute is about the nisab (minimum value), not the type of item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts trivial and quickly-spoiling items from the hadd of theft, but the scope of 'trivial' is disputed within the school.

Amputation for Theft of Perishable and Base Items
Dispute over amputation for stealing quickly-perishing and base items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on amputation for theft of perishable and base items, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab disputes on this issue.

Core Idea
The hand is not cut for trivial, quickly perishing, or base items—punishment is for serious theft, not petty takings.

This passage examines a key controversy in hadd theft: what types of stolen property trigger amputation? While the Quran commands amputation for theft, the Hanafi school refines this through hadith evidence to exempt items that are quickly perishable, of negligible value, or base minerals. We will explore the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi'i, and the proofs they rely upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its basis
State that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not amputate for perishables (milk, meat, moist fruit, dates on tree, watermelon) and base minerals (orpiment, red clay, quicklime). The evidence is the hadith of Aisha and 'no amputation for birds'.
2
Compare with Abu Yusuf's narrower exception
Abu Yusuf only excludes clay, dirt, and dung. He would amputate for other perishables and base minerals unlike Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This intra-Hanafi difference shows a spectrum of opinions
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i universal view
Al-Shafi'i rejects all item-based exceptions, arguing from the general Quranic command. For him, amputation applies regardless of the nature of the property.
4
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil) in detail
The two hadiths are explicit: 'The hand was not cut for a trivial thing' and 'No amputation for birds.' They serve as specific exceptions (takhsis) to the general verse.
Hanafis consider these hadiths as qat'i in indication for these items
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
A thief stealing a watermelon or a fresh chicken would not be amputated under the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad), but would be under al-Shafi'i. Under Abu Yusuf, the chicken theft would also be amputated unless it is clay, dirt, or dung.
This shows how a small difference in proof leads to major practical differences

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السرقة باب ما يقطع به وما لا يقطع وزرنيخ، ومَغَرّة، ونُورة، ولا بما يفسد سريعاً، كلبن، ولحم، وفاكهة رطبة وتمر على شجر، وبطيخ هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وأما عند أبي يوسف لا يُقْطَعُ في كلِّ شيءٍ إلا في الطين، والتراب، والسرقين وعند الشافعي لا يمنع القطع كون الشيء مباح الأصل كالحطب، ولا كونه رطباً، كالفواكه، ولا كونه متعرضاً للفسادِ كالمرقة ولنا : قول عائشة : كانت اليد لا تقطع في عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الشيء التافه وقوله : لا قطع في الطير
The Book of Theft, Chapter: What Amputation is Performed For and What Not. And (no amputation for) orpiment, red clay, quicklime, nor for what quickly spoils, such as milk, meat, moist fruit, dates on the tree, and watermelon. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for Abu Yusuf, amputation is not performed for anything except clay, dirt, and dung. According to al-Shafi'i, amputation is not prevented by the thing being originally permissible like firewood, nor by it being moist like fruits, nor by it being liable to spoilage like soup. And our proof is the saying of Aisha: 'The hand was not amputated during the time of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) for a trivial thing.' And his saying: 'No amputation for birds.'
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the items for which the hand of a thief is amputated. The Hanafi school differentiates based on the nature of the stolen property. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, no amputation is carried out for items like orpiment (a mineral), red clay, quicklime, or anything that spoils quickly – for example, milk, fresh meat, moist fruit (such as quince, pomegranate, apple), dates still on the tree, and watermelon. The rationale is that such items are not typically stored securely or retain value for long. Abu Yusuf holds a stricter view: amputation is not done for anything EXCEPT clay, earth, and dung. This means he would amputate for stealing even perishable items. Al-Shafi'i, on the other hand, rejects all these distinctions; for him, amputation applies regardless of the nature of the item – whether it is originally permissible (like wild grass) or perishable. The Hanafi position is supported by two hadiths: first, the statement of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that during the Prophet's time, the hand was not cut off for a 'trivial' (tafih) item, meaning something of little value. Second, the report that there is 'no amputation for birds' – indicating that small, low-value items are excluded. The commentary further notes that these hadiths are reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah and others, and that the relied-upon Hanafi position is as stated by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
القطع (al-qat'): Amputation of the hand of a thief as a hadd punishment.
التافه (al-tāfih): A thing of negligible value or trivial nature.
الزرنيخ (al-zarnīkh): Orpiment, a yellow arsenic mineral used as pigment or poison.
المَغَرّة (al-magharrah): Red clay, a type of earth used as pigment.
النُورة (al-nūrah): Quicklime, a caustic substance used in plaster.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, which of the following items would NOT incur amputation if stolen?
A) A gold ring
B) Fresh meat
C) A piece of iron
D) A wooden log

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts from hadd amputation theft of quickly-perishing items and base minerals, based on explicit hadith evidence, while the Shafi'i school does not recognize such exemptions.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties

Exempt Items from Theft Amputation: Hanafi Proofs and Madhhab Dispute
Hanafi evidence for not amputating for low-value, perishable, or unharvested items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on exempt items from amputation (qat') and explain the hadith proofs, and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument and the intra-Hanafi difference of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The hand is not cut for low-value, perishable, or unharvested items because specific hadiths limit the general amputation rule.

The hadd punishment for theft is one of the most severe penal laws in Islam. However, the Hanafi school recognizes that not all thefts merit amputation. In this passage, the author defends the school's position that certain categories of property—such as low-value items, birds, unharvested fruit, palm pith, and perishable food—are exempt from the cutting penalty. This lesson unpacks the proofs from hadith and the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school, which holds that only the value threshold matters.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position
Identify the Shafi'i school's stance: amputation is not prevented by the nature of the property (e.g., mubah, moist, perishable). The criteria is purely the value reaching the nisab (quarter dinar).
Shafi'i school emphasizes general proofs like the Quranic verse on amputation.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its core proof
The Hanafi school holds that amputation is not applied to certain items based on their nature. The primary proof is the hadith of Aisha: 'The hand was not cut in the time of the Prophet for a trifling thing (al-shay' al-tafih).' Understand that 'tafih' refers to low-value items, determined by 'urf.
This hadith is mawquf but considered sufficient evidence.
3
Examine additional hadith proofs for specific items
The passage provides separate proofs for birds, fruit, palm pith (kathar), and perishable food. For each, note whether the hadith is marfu' or mawquf, and how it supports the exemption.
The hadith on birds is mawquf from Uthman and the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf, in Nawadir Hisham, is reported to amputate for everything except dirt and dung, and in al-Haruniyyat he excluded wood. Contrast this with the Zahir al-Riwayah position that exempts the listed items. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
This shows that not all Hanafi imams agreed on the scope of exemptions.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a Hanafi judge would not amputate for stealing a bird from an open field, while a Shafi'i judge might if its value reaches a quarter dinar. This illustrates how a single fiqh principle can yield different outcomes across madhahib.
The dispute is legitimate (ikhtilaf sahih) and both views have strong proofs.

[١] قوله : في ثمر ؛ - بالثاء المثلثة - ، والمراد به المعلّق على الشجر، يدلّ عليه ما أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجة عن عبد الله بن عمرو : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن النمر المعلق فقال : من أصاب فيه من ذي حاجة غير متخذ خبيئة فلا شيء عليه ، ومن سرق منه شيئاً بعد أن يؤديه الجرين (٦) فبلغ ثمن المجنّ فعليه القطع)))
And according to al-Shafi'i, the fact that something is originally permissible like wood does not prevent amputation, nor that it is moist like fruits, nor that it is subject to spoilage like soup. And our proof: the statement of Aisha: 'The hand was not cut in the time of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, for a trifling thing' i.e., insignificant. And his statement: 'No amputation for birds'. Al-Ayni said in al-Binaya: 'From Abu Yusuf in Nawadir Hisham: He said: I amputate for everything stolen except for dirt and dung. He said in Sharh al-Aqta': That is the statement of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, and his companions have two opinions regarding water and dirt. And it is related from Abu Yusuf in al-Haruniyyat: I amputate for everything except for wood.' [1] His statement: 'And our proof...' etc.; know that there have come reports, both marfu' and mawquf, which collectively support our school. Among them is what Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from Hisham ibn Urwa from Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, who said: 'The hand of the thief was not cut in the time of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, for a trifling thing, nor was it cut for less than the price of a shield or a buckler.' And similar was narrated by Abd al-Razzaq in his Musannaf and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh in his Musnad, as mentioned by al-Zaylai and others. And among them is the statement of Uthman: 'No amputation for birds,' narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba and Abd al-Razzaq. And Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid: 'I have not seen anyone amputate for a bird.' ... (footnotes)... Book of Theft, Chapter: What is amputated for and what is not amputated for. And his statement: 'No amputation for fruit nor for palm pith.' And al-Bayhaqi narrated from Abu al-Darda: 'There is no amputation for the thief of pigeons.' And the author of al-Hidaya and the commentator relied on the hadith: 'No amputation for birds,' but it is not found marfu'; it is only mawquf from the Companions, and that is sufficient in this chapter. And among them is the hadith: 'No amputation for fruit nor for kathar' – with two fatha – meaning the pith of the palm, also called palm fat. It was narrated by al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Abi Shayba, Malik, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Ahmad, al-Darimi, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh and others. And the commentator mentioned instead of kathar: 'tree', but it is not found marfu'. And that hadith: 'Indeed I do not amputate for food,' narrated by Abu Dawud in al-Marasil marfu', and similar was narrated by Abd al-Razzaq and Ibn Abi Shayba: 'The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was brought a man who stole food and he did not amputate him.' Sufyan al-Thawri, one of its narrators, said: It is the food that spoils within the same day, such as tharid and meat. And the author of al-Hidaya mentioned this hadith with the wording: 'No amputation for food,' but the extractors of its hadiths did not find it. [1] His statement: 'in fruit' – with thā'-i muthallatha – and what is meant is that which hangs on the tree. This is indicated by what Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah narrated from Abdullah ibn Amr: 'The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was asked about hanging fruit, and he said: Whoever takes from it out of need without concealing it, there is nothing on him; and whoever steals something from it after it has been taken to the threshing floor and it reaches the price of a shield, then amputation is due.'
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and intra-Hanafi difference regarding the conditions for amputation (qat') of the thief's hand. The Shafi'i school holds that the hand is cut for theft of any item of value, regardless of its nature, including wood, moist fruits, or perishable items. The Hanafi school disagrees and presents multiple proofs to show that amputation is not applied to certain categories of items. The commentary begins by quoting the Shafi'i position then immediately gives the Hanafi proof: the statement of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that the hand was not cut in the Prophet's time for insignificant (tafih) items. Then the hadith 'no amputation for birds' is cited. The commentator al-Ayni then reports an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf, in one narration, says he would amputate for anything stolen except dirt and dung, aligning with the Shafi'i view in certain cases. But in another narration, he excludes wood. Then the commentary reviews the specific proofs: the marfu' and mawquf hadiths that collectively support the Hanafi school. The text from Aisha is detailed, and the statement of Uthman and the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz are cited to show that amputation was not applied for birds. Moving to page 287, the chapter heading is given, and the discussion continues with the hadith 'no amputation for fruit nor for palm pith' (or tree in another version). Al-Bayhaqi's narration from Abu al-Darda exempts pigeons. The author notes that some hadiths used by the school are mawquf, not marfu', but that is sufficient. Then the hadith 'no amputation for food' is discussed, with an explanation that food that spoils quickly (like bread dipped in broth and meat) is exempt. Finally, the passage clarifies that 'fruit' means fruit still hanging on the tree, citing a hadith from Abdullah ibn Amr that distinguishes between taking out of need and stealing after it reaches the threshing floor.

Key Terms
قطع (qat'): Amputation of the hand as a hadd punishment for theft.
التافه (al-tāfih): Insignificant or low-value property, which does not trigger amputation.
الطير (al-ṭayr): Birds, which are not subject to amputation when stolen, according to Hanafi school.
ثمر (thamar): Fruit still hanging on the tree, not yet harvested, exempt from amputation.
كثر (kathar): The pith (heart) of the palm tree, also known as 'shahm al-nakhl'.
طعام (ṭa'ām): Food that spoils quickly, such as prepared dishes, exempt from amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following items is NOT exempt from amputation (qat') based on the proofs in this passage?
A) A bunch of dates still hanging on a tree
B) A live bird stolen from an open field
C) A wooden plank stolen from a construction site
D) A bowl of soup left unattended

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts low-value, unharvested, and perishable items from theft amputation based on specific hadiths that qualify the general command to amputate.

No Amputation for Unharvested Fruits and Trees
Explains the exception to theft punishment for hanging fruits and trees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi exception to amputation for theft of unharvested fruits and trees, identify the key hadith evidence, and explain the condition that lifts the exemption.

Core Idea
No amputation for fruits on the tree or standing trees — theft lacks hirz.

This passage from the Book of Theft clarifies a key exception in Islamic criminal law. While theft of secured property warrants the hadd punishment of amputation, the Hanafi school holds that fruits still hanging on trees and standing trees are not subject to this penalty. The reason is that such items are not considered properly secured (hirz). The commentary provides several hadith proofs, including the famous distinction between hanging dates and dates brought to the threshing floor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the exemption
What is being exempted from amputation? Fruits still hanging on the tree and standing trees themselves.
2
Locate the key hadith evidence
Which hadith is cited as the main proof? The hadith of Abdullah ibn Amr about hanging dates, reported by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
What is the operative principle (ratio legis)? The lack of hirz (secure storage) – unharvested fruits are not considered guarded.
4
Distinguish the boundary
When does amputation apply despite the exemption? After harvest and when the fruits are taken to the threshing floor (jarin), and the value reaches the price of a shield.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Which schools disagree and on what basis? Some other schools may require amputation even for hanging fruits if the value threshold is met, but the Hanafi school relies on the explicit hadith evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله : في ثمر ؛ - بالثاء المثلثة - ، والمراد به المعلّق على الشجر، يدلّ عليه ما أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجة عن عبد الله بن عمرو : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن النمر المعلق فقال : من أصاب فيه من ذي حاجة غير متخذ خبيئة فلا شيء عليه ، ومن سرق منه شيئاً بعد أن يؤديه الجرين فبلغ ثمن المجنّ فعليه القطع»
Book of Theft, Chapter: What is amputated for and what is not. And His statement: 'No amputation for fruit and tree'. And al-Bayhaqi narrated from Abu al-Darda', he said: 'There is no amputation for the thief of pigeons'. And the author of al-Hidayah and the commentator followed this with the hadith: 'No amputation for birds', raised (marfu'), but it was not found; rather it is stopped (mawquf) on the Companions, and that is sufficient in this chapter. And from that is the hadith: 'No amputation for fruit and kathar' – with two fatha's – it is the spathe (jumar), also called fat of the palm. Al-Tirmidhi, Ibn Abi Shaybah, Malik, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Ahmad, al-Darimi, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh and others narrated it. And the commentator substituted 'shajar' (tree) for 'kathar', but it was not found raised. And that hadith: 'Indeed I do not amputate for food', narrated by Abu Dawud in al-Marasil raised, and similarly narrated by Abd al-Razzaq and Ibn Abi Shaybah: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) was brought a man who stole food, and he did not amputate him'. Sufyan al-Thawri, one of its narrators, said: 'It is food that spoils during the day, like tharid (bread soaked in broth) and meat'. The author of al-Hidayah mentioned this hadith with the wording: 'No amputation for food', but the compilers of his hadith did not find it. [1] His statement: 'in fruit' – with a triple-pointed thā – and what is meant is that which is hanging on the tree. This is indicated by what Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah narrated from Abdullah ibn Amr: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about hanging dates. He said: Whoever takes from it out of need without hiding it away, there is nothing on him. But whoever steals from it after it is taken to the threshing floor (jarin) and it reaches the price of a shield, then amputation is due.'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Book of Theft. It establishes that amputation (hadd) is not applied for stealing fruits still hanging on trees or for standing trees. The commentary cites several ahadith to support this, some raised (marfu') and some narrated as statements of the Companions (mawquf). The key evidence is the hadith reported by the Companions that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about hanging dates and made a distinction: if a needy person takes a little without hiding, no penalty; but if after harvest (when the dates are in the threshing floor) someone steals enough to reach the price of a shield, then amputation is due. Thus, the exemption applies only before the crop is harvested and guarded. The commentary also clarifies that the term 'thamar' (fruit) here refers specifically to fruit still on the tree, not yet harvested. The basic principle is that amputation requires the stolen property to be in a secure place (hirz); unharvested fruits and trees are not considered properly guarded, hence no cut.

Key Terms
ثمر (thamar): Fruit hanging on the tree, not yet harvested.
حرز (hirz): A secure place where property is kept safe. The lack of hirz is the reason for no amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith of Abdullah ibn Amr cited in the text, which of the following triggers amputation for stealing dates?
A) Stealing hanging dates of any value.
B) Stealing dates after they have been taken to the threshing floor and value reaches shield price.
C) Stealing dates from a guarded orchard.
D) Stealing any type of fruit from a tree.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts unharvested fruits and standing trees from amputation due to lack of hirz, as established by the hadith of hanging dates.

Exceptions to Hadd for Theft: Crops, Intoxicants, Instruments, and Crosses
Listing exemptions to amputation due to missing conditions or shubha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of items exempt from amputation for theft in Hanafi fiqh and explain the legal reason (lack of hirz or shubha) for each exemption.

Core Idea
Hudud are warded off by doubts; four items are exempt due to missing conditions or shubha.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from the Hanafi tradition that specifies items whose theft does not incur the prescribed punishment of amputation. The passage covers unharvested crops, intoxicating drinks, musical instruments, and crosses. Understanding the reasoning behind each exemption sharpens your grasp of how conditions (shurut) and doubts (shubuhah) operate in criminal law.

Guided Path
1
List the four exempt items
Identify each item from the text: unharvested crops (zar' lam yuhsad), intoxicating drinks (ashriba mutriba), instruments of amusement (alat lahw), and crosses of gold or silver (salib min dhahab aw fidda).
The text explicitly mentions these four.
2
Determine the reason for exemption for each
For crops: lack of proper guarding (hirz). For drinks: twofold – they are perishable and there is a shubha that the taker may be disposing of it. For instruments: shubha due to possibility of forbidding evil or that they are not considered property. For crosses: not lawful property for Muslims.
The commentary unpacks each reason.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on instruments
Sahibayn hold instruments are not property (ghayr mutaqawwim) so no guarantee and no hadd. Abu Hanifa holds they are property but taking them may be an act of forbidding evil, creating shubha that drops the hadd.
Both views agree on dropping the hadd.
4
Connect to the principle that hudud are warded off by doubts
The commentary explicitly states: 'al-hudud tudra'u bi adna shubha.' This is the overarching legal maxim that explains why these items are exempt.
This principle is central to Hanafi criminal law.
5
Appreciate the practical outcome
In all cases, no amputation is applied. However, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) may still be possible, and the items themselves may be unlawful to possess or consume.
The exemption is only from the fixed hadd.

وزرع لم يحصد) ؛ لعدم الحرز. (ولا في أشربة مطربة، وآلات لهو، وصليب من ذهب، أو فضة) [١] قوله : لم يُحصد ؛ مجهول من الحصاد - بالفتح - وهو : قطع الزرع ، يعني لا يقطع بسرقة زرع لم يبلغ الحصاد ؛ لعدم وجود الحرز ، فإذا حصد وأحرز، ففي سرقته القطع ، والأصل في هذا الباب هو ما مر من الأحاديث. [٢] قوله : أشربة ؛ هو - بفتح الهمزة ، وكسر الراء المهملة، بينهما شين معجمة ، جمع الشراب، بمعنى ما يشرب. والمطربة على صيغة اسم الفاعل من الإطراب، والمراد المسكرة، فإنّ الطّرب عبارة عن خفة العقل من شدة حزن وجزع ، أو شدّة سرور توجب صدور ما لا يصدرُ من العاقل. وليس قيد الطرب احترازياً ؛ فإنّه لا قطع في سرقة الأشربة مطلقاً، أما إذا لم تكن مطربةً فلكونها مما يتسارع إليه الفساد، وأما إذا كانت مطربة فلهذا الوجه أيضاً، ولاحتمال أن يناول السّارِقُ الآخذ للإراق، فيكون أخذها من باب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر ، لا من باب السرقة، ووجود هذا الاحتمال يسقط الحد ؛ لأنّ الحدود تدرأ بأدنى شبهة. [٣]قوله : وآلات لهو ؛ أي التي تستعمل في مجالس اللهو والغناء كالطبل والطنبور وغير ذلك، ولو كان طبل الغزاة ؛ لأنّ صلاحية اللهو صارت مشبهة. والوجه : أ : أنّ آلات اللهو غير متقوّمة عندهما حتى لا يضمن متلفها، وعنده وإن كان يجب الضمان على المتلف لكن في أخذها شبهةُ النّهي عن المنكر، والشبهة دارئة للحد، كذا في «الفتح»، وغيره. [4] قوله : وصليب ؛ على وزن فعيل ، وهو بهيئة خطين متقاطعين تصوّره النصارى وتعبده وتظن أنه هيئة الصليب الذي صلب عليه سيدنا عيسى على نبينا وعليه السلام، وقد كذبوا فيما زعموا ، وَمَا قَتَلُوهُ وَمَا صَلَبُوهُ وَلَكِن شُبّهَ لَهُمْ ، وَمَا قَتَلُوهُ يقينا ) بَل رَفَعَهُ الله إِليه
And unharvested crops – due to lack of guarding. And not in intoxicating drinks, instruments of amusement, and a cross of gold or silver. [1] His statement: 'has not been harvested' – passive from harvest – meaning cutting of crops, i.e. there is no amputation for stealing crops that have not reached harvest; due to absence of guarding. So when harvested and guarded, then there is amputation for its theft. The basis in this chapter is the previously mentioned ahadith. [2] His statement: 'drinks' – with fatha on the hamza, kasra on the ra, a shin between them; plural of sharab, meaning what is drunk. 'Mutriba' is on the pattern of the active participle from itrab, meaning intoxicating, for tarab is lightness of the mind from severe sadness and distress, or severe joy causing what a sane person would not do. The condition of 'mutriba' is not exclusionary; indeed there is no amputation for theft of drinks absolutely. If they are not intoxicating, it is because they quickly spoil. If they are intoxicating, for this reason also, and because the thief might be taking it to pour it out, so his taking is from the category of enjoining good and forbidding evil, not from theft. This possibility drops the hadd, because hudud are warded off by the slightest doubt. [3] His statement: 'instruments of amusement' – those used in gatherings of amusement and singing, like the drum and tanbur and so on, even if it is a battle drum; because its suitability for amusement creates a doubt. The view is that instruments of amusement are not considered property according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), so their destroyer is not liable for guarantee. According to him (Abu Hanifa), even though the destroyer must guarantee, in taking them there is a doubt of forbidding evil, and doubt repels the hadd, as in al-Fath and others. [4] His statement: 'cross' – on the pattern of fu'ayl, and it is the shape of two intersecting lines that Christians depict and worship, thinking it is the shape of the cross on which our master Jesus (peace be upon our Prophet and upon him) was crucified. They have lied in their claim: 'And they killed him not, nor crucified him, but it was made to appear so to them, and indeed they killed him not, but Allah raised him to Himself.'
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage details additional exceptions to the hadd of theft. First, unharvested crops (zar' lam yuhsad) are exempt because they are not properly guarded. Once harvested and stored, amputation applies. Second, intoxicating drinks (ashriba mutriba) are exempt for two reasons: (a) they are perishable, and (b) there is a possibility the thief intended to discard it as an act of forbidding evil, creating a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd. The commentary notes that this exemption applies to all drinks, intoxicating or not, due to the doubt. Third, instruments of amusement (alat lahw) are exempt. Here there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, such instruments are not considered valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim), so no guarantee is due; Abu Hanifa holds they are property and guarantee is due, but the act of taking them can be motivated by forbidding evil, creating doubt. On both views, the hadd is dropped. Fourth, crosses made of gold or silver (salib min dhahab aw fidda) are exempt because they are associated with Christian worship and are not considered lawful property for Muslims. The commentary also quotes the Quranic verse denying the crucifixion of Jesus, to emphasize the falsehood of the cross.

Key Terms
أشربة مطربة (ashriba mutriba): Intoxicating drinks; the term 'mutriba' indicates that which causes tarab (lightness of mind due to strong emotion).
آلات لهو (alat lahw): Musical instruments and instruments of amusement used in gatherings of leisure.
صليب (salib): A cross, particularly made of gold or silver, used in Christian worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the text say there is no amputation for stealing intoxicating drinks?
A) Because intoxicating drinks are not valuable.
B) Because there is a doubt that the thief may have taken them to pour them out, thereby enjoining good.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly said so.
D) Because intoxicating drinks are not considered property in Islam.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts four categories from the hadd of amputation for theft, based on the fundamental principle that hudud are warded off by doubts.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate

Items Exempt from Hand Amputation in Theft: Seven Categories
Lists seven items with explanation of why hadd is not applied

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven categories of stolen items for which the Hanafi school does not apply hand amputation and explain the role of shubha and hirs in each exception.

Core Idea
Hadd punishments are averted by the slightest doubt; thus, seven specific items avoid amputation due to lack of hirs or shubha.

The Hanafi school imposes strict conditions for applying the fixed punishment (hadd) of hand amputation for theft. Among these conditions are that the stolen property must be taken from a secure place (hirs) and that there must be no legal doubt (shubha). This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, enumerates seven items where the hadd is not applied, detailing the reasoning for each. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying Islamic criminal law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Seven Items
List each item mentioned in the passage: unharvested crops, intoxicating drinks, instruments of amusement, cross of gold or silver, chess and backgammon, mosque door, and mus'haf (Quranic manuscript). Note that the text explicitly numbers them through commentary.
The first item (unharvested crops) is mentioned twice in the matn but is a single category.
2
Analyze the Reasoning for Each
For each item, determine whether the exemption is due to lack of proper safeguarding (hirs) or due to a doubt (shubha) that averts the hadd. Unharvested crops lack hirs; intoxicating drinks have shubha of disposal; instruments of amusement have shubha of forbidding evil; cross and chess have shubha of breaking; mosque door lacks hirs; mus'haf has shubha of preservation.
The overarching principle is 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hadd punishments are averted by doubts).
3
Examine Intra-Hanafi Differences
Note that for instruments of amusement, Abu Hanifa considers them valuable property, while the Sahibayn do not. Despite this difference, all agree that no cutting applies due to shubha. This shows that the doubt principle overrides other considerations.
The text references al-Fath and al-Bahr for the shubha reasoning.
4
Identify Cross-Madhhab Dispute
The passage mentions that the Shafi'i school disagrees on the mosque door. For Hanafis, the mosque door is not considered properly safeguarded because the mosque is a public space lacking enclosure.
This is the only explicit cross-madhhab ikhtilaf in the passage.
5
Apply the Principle to Analogous Cases
Extend the reasoning to similar cases mentioned in the commentary: sandals of worshippers in the mosque, curtain of the Ka'ba, and any door of a house (if not locked). These also lack hirs or have shubha, so no cutting applies.
The text from al-Bahr confirms these extensions.

And unharvested crops, nor in intoxicating drinks, and instruments of amusement, and a cross of gold or silver, and unharvested crops: due to lack of proper safeguarding. [2] (Nor in intoxicating drinks, instruments of amusement, and a cross of gold or silver. [1] His saying: 'unharvested' (lam yuhsad) is passive from al-hisad (with fat-h), which is cutting the crop, meaning the hand is not cut for stealing a crop that has not reached harvest because there is no proper safeguarding. When it is harvested and safeguarded, then cutting applies in its theft, and the basis in this chapter is what has passed from hadiths. [2] His saying: 'ashriba' (drinks) is with fat-h on the hamza and kasr on the ra', with a shin between them, plural of sharab, meaning what is drunk. 'Mutriba' is on the pattern of the active participle from itrab, meaning intoxicating, for tarab is lightening of the mind from intense grief and distress, or intense joy that causes one to do what a rational person would not. The restriction of tarab is not exclusionary; indeed there is no cutting in stealing drinks absolutely. As for when they are not intoxicating, it is because they are things that spoil quickly, and when they are intoxicating, it is also for this reason, and due to the possibility that the thief might say 'I took it for disposal,' so his taking it would be from the category of enjoining good and forbidding evil, not from theft, and the existence of this possibility drops the hadd, because hadd punishments are averted by the slightest doubt. [3] His saying: 'and instruments of amusement' (alat lahw): that which is used in gatherings of amusement and singing, like the drum and the tanbur and others, even if it is the drum of warriors, because its suitability for amusement creates doubt. The reasoning: that instruments of amusement are not considered valuable property according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] so that their destroyer does not guarantee them, and according to [Abu Hanifa] although guarantee is required upon the destroyer, in taking them there is the doubt of forbidding evil, and doubt averts the hadd, as in al-Fath and others. [4] His saying: 'and a cross' (salib): on the pattern of fa'il, and it is the figure of two intersecting lines that the Christians imagine and worship, thinking it is the figure of the cross on which our master Jesus (peace be upon our Prophet and him) was crucified, and they lied in what they claimed, 'and they did not kill him nor did they crucify him, but it was made to appear so to them' (Qur'an 4:157), 'and they did not kill him with certainty' (4:157), 'rather Allah raised him to Himself' (4:158). Or chess and backgammon (nard): because he says 'I took it for disposal and breaking.' And a mosque door: due to lack of proper safeguarding, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And a mus'haf (copy of the Quran).
[1] His saying: 'and chess and backgammon': even though they fall under instruments of amusement, but their mention is for clarification and explicitness, because most of what comes to mind from instruments of amusement is flutes... [2] His saying: 'he says': meaning it is possible that he says that, for the existence of his saying is not a condition for aversion. [3] His saying: 'and breaking': this is connected to instruments of amusement, the cross, chess, and backgammon. [4] His saying: 'and a mosque door': and likewise the thief of any door of a house due to lack of proper safeguarding, and likewise the thief of sandals of worshippers from the mosque, and likewise the curtain of the Ka'ba, as in al-Bahr. [0] His saying: 'and a mus'haf': with fat-h on the ha' and three possible vowels on the mim, with damm being most famous, as in al-Misbah, and it is what the Quran is written in. The reasoning for no cutting is the existence of doubt that averts the hadd, because it is possible that he might say...
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Wiqayah explains the types of stolen property for which the thief is not subject to hand amputation (had al-sariqa). The author, a Hanafi scholar, enumerates seven categories and provides the legal reasoning (ta'lil) for each exception. The overarching principle is that hadd punishments are averted by doubts (shubuhat) and, in the case of crops, due to lack of proper safeguarding (hirs). The first item is unharvested crops (zar' lam yuhsad): because until harvest, the crop is not considered properly safeguarded; once harvested and stored, amputation applies. Second, intoxicating drinks (ashriba mutriba): no amputation for stealing any drink, whether intoxicating or not. For non-intoxicating drinks, it is because they spoil quickly and are not considered valuable property subject to amputation. For intoxicating drinks, additionally, there is the doubt that the taker may have intended to dispose of the prohibited substance, thus acting in commanding good and forbidding evil, which removes the criminal intent of theft. The hadith principle 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hadd punishments are averted by doubts) applies. Third, instruments of amusement (alat lahw) like drums and tanburs: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), these items are not considered valuable property (muttaqawwim) at all, so there is no hand amputation even according to Abu Hanifa, who deems them property, because taking them with the intention of destroying them (to prevent evil) creates a doubt. Fourth, a cross of gold or silver: since it is an object of idolatry, the taker may claim he took it to break it and remove evil. Fifth, chess and backgammon: even though they fall under instruments of amusement, they are explicitly mentioned because the common understanding of amusement instruments refers to musical instruments. The same reasoning of potential disposal applies. Sixth, a mosque door: because a mosque is a public place and the door is not considered in a secure location (hirs); similarly, sandals of worshippers and the curtain of the Ka'ba. This is contrary to the Shafi'i school, which may deem such items as properly safeguarded. Seventh, a mus'haf (Quranic manuscript): the absence of amputation is due to the doubt that the taker might claim he took it to preserve it or for a religious purpose. The passage emphasizes that the thief's actual statement is not required; the mere possibility suffices to avert the hadd. The footnote quotes Fath al-Qadir and clarifies the cross issue with Quranic verses that Jesus was not crucified.

Key Terms
حرز (hirs): Proper safeguarding; the condition that stolen property must be in a secure place for the hadd to apply.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or legal uncertainty that prevents the application of hadd punishment.
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law, such as amputation for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is hand amputation not applied for stealing intoxicating drinks in the Hanafi school?
A) Because intoxicating drinks are not valuable property.
B) Because there is a doubt that the taker may have intended to dispose of it, thus acting in forbidding evil.
C) Because the stolen amount does not reach the nisab.
D) Because the drink was not properly sealed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts seven categories of stolen items from hand amputation based on the principles of lack of proper safeguarding (hirs) or the presence of legal doubt (shubha).

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate

When Hand Cutting Is Not Applied: Theft of a Free Child, Mushaf with Ornaments, Adult Slave, and Notebook
States conditions when cutting is not applied for theft of specific items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of items for which hand cutting is not applied in Hanafi law and explain the reason for each.

Core Idea
Theft cutting is only for property taken by stealth; free humans, mushafs, adult slaves, and notebooks (except accounts) are excluded.

This passage from the Hidayah clarifies the scope of the hadd penalty for theft. While theft of property meeting certain conditions incurs hand cutting, the law exempts several items either because they are not considered property, or because the method of taking does not constitute theft (sariqa). We will examine each exemption and the reasoning behind it, including an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ornaments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exempted items
List the five items mentioned in the matn: free child, mushaf, slave, notebook, and note the exception for small slave and account book.
The matn states: 'A free child, and even if they have ornaments, and a slave and a notebook except the small [child], and an account book.'
2
Analyze the basis for each exemption
For each item, determine whether the exemption is due to the item not being considered property (mal) or due to the manner of taking not being theft (sariqa). Free child and mushaf: not property. Adult slave: taking is usurpation or deception, not theft. Notebook: purpose is content (knowledge), not the paper itself.
The commentary explains that for an adult slave, the taking is ghasb or khida', both outside the definition of sariqa.
3
Understand the ornament dispute
Note the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding ornaments on the mushaf or child. Abu Hanifa: ornaments are subsidiary (tab') to the non-property original, so no cutting. Abu Yusuf: if ornaments alone reach nisab, cutting applies.
The principle 'al-tabi' tabi' (the subsidiary follows the original) governs Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Apply the exceptions
Recognize that small slaves and account books are exceptions: small slaves are property and can be stolen by stealth; account books are taken for the money recorded (property).
Small slave: cutting applies. Account book: cutting applies.
5
Synthesize the overall ruling
Summarize the rule: The hadd of cutting is only for theft of movable property (mal) taken stealthily from its safe place. Items that are not property, or whose taking does not meet the definition of sariqa, are exempt. The subsidiary principle and the purpose of taking are key considerations.

لأنه يقولُ أخذتُهُ للقراءة خلافا لأبي يوسف والشَّافِعِي ، وصبي .
لأنه ليس بمال، (ولو محلّيين ) ، يرجع إلى المصحف والصبي، فإن الحلية تبع". وعند أبي يوسف له إن بلغت الحلية النصاب يقطع.
وعبد ودفتر ت (٤) (۲) إلا الصغير، ودفتر الحساب ؛ لأنَّ أخذ العبد الكبير يكونُ غصباً أو خداعاً لا سرقة، والمقصود من الدفتر ما فيه، وهو ليس بمال أخذته لأن اقرأ منه القرآن، وأمّا الكتب العلمية، فإن كانت شرعيّة فأخذها كأخذ آلات اللهو.
[1]قوله : وصبي : أي لا قطع بسرقة صبي حر لعدم كونه مالاً ، وكذا البالغ الحر بالطريق الأولى، وأما سرقة العبد فتوجب القطع إذا كان صغيراً، ولا توجب إذا كان كبيراً ؛ لأن العبد مال غير أنّ أخذ الكبير ليس بسرقة ، بل غصب أو خداع على ما سيأتي ، والمراد بالكبير هناك المميز الذي يعبر عن نفسه بالغاً كان أو غير بالغ، وبالصغير خلافه. كذا في «البحر». [٢] قوله : ولو محليين ؛ هذا اللفظ في بعض النسخ باليائين، والصواب ما في بعض النسخ محلين بالياء الواحدة : أي لا قطع بسرقة المصحف والصبي، ولو كان عليهما حلية ذهب، أو فضة.
[٣]قوله : تبع ؛ فحيث لم يجب القطعُ في سرقة الأصل لم يجب بالتبع.
[٤]قوله : ودفتر؛ هو - بالفتح - ، وقد يكسر : جماعة الصحف المضمومة، والحاصل أنه لا قطع بسرقة عبد إلا إذا كان صغيراً ، فإنّ أخذ العبد الكبير إن كان أخذه قهراً فهو غصب، وإن أخذه بحلية، فهو خداع، وكل منهما ليس بسرقة.
وكذا : لا قطع بسرقة دفتر ؛ لأن المقصود من أخذها هو معرفة ما كتب فيه، لا نفس أوراق القرطاس، وهو ليس بمال إلا إذا كان دفتر الحساب، فإنّ المقصود من أخذه المالُ الذي كَتَبَ حاسبه فيه.
(1) في حاشيتا قليوبي وعميرة (٤) : (١٨٧ يعتبر القطع في المصحف إذا بلغت قيمته دينار ربع و«التجريد لنفع العبيد» (٤) : ٢٢٠) ، و«فتوحات الوهاب» (٥ : ١٤١) : لا قطع بسرقة مصحف وقف للقراءة في المسجد.
ذهب، وفي «تحفة المحتاج»(۹: ۱۳۲)
(٢) أي سواء كان فيه علم الشريعة أو الشعر أ أو اللغة ؛ لأن المقصود من دفاتر هذه الأشياء ما فيها،
وهو ليس بمال. ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(٣ : ٢٤٤).
A free child, and even if they have ornaments, and a slave and a notebook except the small [child], and an account book. [1] Because he says: I took it for recitation, contrary to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i. And a child because it is not property. (And even if they have ornaments) refers to the mushaf and the child, for the ornament is a subsidiary. And according to Abu Yusuf, if the ornament reaches the nisab, cutting is applied. And a slave and a notebook (4) (2) except the small [child] and an account book; because taking an adult slave is usurpation or deception, not theft, and the purpose of the notebook is its content, which is not property. I took it to read the Quran from it. As for scientific books, if they are religious, taking them is like taking musical instruments. [1] His statement: 'and a child' means no cutting for stealing a free child because he is not property, and similarly a free adult a fortiori. As for stealing a slave, it necessitates cutting if [the slave] is small, and does not necessitate if [the slave] is large; because the slave is property, but taking an adult is not theft, rather usurpation or deception as will come. What is meant by 'large' there is the discerning one who can express himself, whether adult or not, and by 'small' the opposite. So in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: 'and even if they have ornaments' — this wording in some copies is with two ya's, and the correct is what is in some copies: 'muhallayn' with one ya, meaning no cutting for stealing the mushaf and the child, even if they have ornaments of gold or silver. [3] His statement: 'subsidiary' — so since cutting is not obligatory in the theft of the original, it is not obligatory by subsidiary. [4] His statement: 'a notebook' — it is with fatha, and may be kasra: a collection of bound pages. The summary is that there is no cutting for stealing a slave unless [the slave] is small. For taking an adult slave, if he took him by force it is usurpation, and if he took him with a trick it is deception, and both are not theft. Likewise: no cutting for stealing a notebook because the purpose of taking it is to know what is written in it, not the paper itself, and it is not property except if it is an account book, for the purpose of taking it is the money that its accountant wrote in it. (1) In Hashiyatay Qalyubi wa 'Umayra (4): (187) Cutting is considered for the mushaf if its value reaches a quarter dinar, and 'al-Tajrid li-Naf' al-'Ubayd' (4:220), and 'Futuhat al-Wahhab' (5:141): no cutting for stealing a mushaf that is a waqf for recitation in the mosque. Gold, and in 'Tuhfat al-Muhtaj' (9:132). (2) Meaning whether it contains knowledge of Shari'a or poetry or language; because the purpose of these notebooks is their content, which is not property. See: Fath Bab al-'Inaya (3:244).
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on theft, specifically what necessitates and does not necessitate hand cutting. The author states that hand cutting is not applied for stealing a free child (whether adult or minor, because a free human is not considered property), a mushaf (Quran) even if it has gold or silver ornaments (because the ornaments are subsidiary to the non-property mushaf), an adult slave (because taking an adult slave is considered usurpation or deception, not theft), and a notebook (because the purpose is the content, not the paper, except for an account book where the purpose is the money recorded). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Yusuf, if the ornaments on the mushaf or child reach the nisab, cutting is applied. The author also notes that if the notebook contains religious knowledge, taking it is like taking musical instruments (i.e., not subject to theft cutting). The commentary follows the order of the matn, explaining each item. The key principle is that the cut is only for theft of property (mal) with certain conditions, and these items either lack property status or the manner of taking falls outside the definition of theft.

Key Terms
محليين (muhallayn): According to the correct reading (with one ya), it means 'with ornaments' (gold or silver) on the mushaf or child.
تبع (tab'): Subsidiary or follower; here it means the ornament is subsidiary to the original object (mushaf/child) in terms of legal status.
خداع (khida'): Deception or trickery; taking something by deceit rather than by stealth or force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (following Abu Hanifa), is hand cutting applied for stealing a mushaf (copy of the Quran) that has silver ornaments worth more than the nisab?
A) Yes, because the silver is property and reaches nisab.
B) No, because the ornaments are subsidiary to the mushaf, which is not property.
C) Yes, only if the mushaf is a waqf.
D) No, because the mushaf is not property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the hadd for theft is not applied when the stolen item is not property in the relevant sense (free human, mushaf) or when the taking is not true theft (adult slave, non-account notebook); the ornament dispute illustrates the principle that the subsidiary follows the original (al-tabi' tabi').

No Cutting for Stealing a Dog or Leopard
Lists dog and leopard among items not subject to hadd for theft.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stealing a dog or leopard and explain how doubt (shubha) in property status prevents the hadd.

Core Idea
Doubt in property status (shubha fi al-maliyyah) drops the hadd, even if the dog is beneficial and owned.

The chapter on theft (hudud) specifies that hand cutting is not applied for stealing certain items. Among these are dogs and leopards. This lesson examines the Hanafi reasoning: the animal's species is inherently permissible (mubah) in Dar al-Islam, and there is scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) on its property value. This combination creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the application of the severe hadd penalty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items and the ruling
Note that the text explicitly states no cutting (la qat') for dog and leopard, even if they are owned property.
The ruling is a clear exception to the general rule of cutting for theft.
2
Understand the first reason: mubah origin
The species of these animals is found free and permissible in the land of Islam. Thus they are considered insignificant (tafih), as one could simply catch a wild one.
This is a Hanafi specific argument linking to the minimum value requirement for hadd.
3
Recognize the second reason: ikhtilaf on maliyyah
Scholars differ on whether dogs have property value. Shafi'i, Hanbali, and Malik (in one view) deny property value; Hanafis affirm it for beneficial dogs. This dispute creates a shubha that drops the hadd.
The shubha prevents the certainty required for hadd punishment.
4
Compare cross-madhhab positions
Note that other schools that deny property value would also not apply cutting, but for a different reason (no property). The Hanafi ruling is unique in that it acknowledges property yet still withholds the hadd due to shubha.
This illustrates how ikhtilaf itself can be a mitigating factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في كلب، وفهد، وخيانة، وخَلْس، ونهب
[1] قوله : المال ; لأن الغرض من أخذه هو أخذ المال، فمن كتب اسمه فيه مثلاً.
[٢]قوله : ولا في كلب وفهد ; أي لا يقطعُ بسرقة كلب وفهد مملوكين لرجل ; لأن جنس هذين الحيوانين يوجد مباحاً في دار الإسلام، فيدخل في التافه. وأيضاً : اختلفوا في ماليّة الكلب، فعند الشافعي وأحمد ومالك في رواية لا مالية ; ولهذا لم يجوزوا بيعه، وعند أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه وإبراهيم النخعي له يجوز بيع الكلاب التي ينتفع بها، وهو دليل المالية، وهذا الاختلاف أورث شبهة في المالية، فاسقطت الحدّ ، كذا في «البناية».
[٣] قوله : وخيانة ; أي لا قطع في الخيانة : وهي أن يخون المودع فيما في يده من مال المودع ، فإن الخيانة غير السرقة كما أن الانتهاب والاختلاس ليس بداخل في السرقة فلا قطع بهما أيضاً; لحديث: ليس على خائن ولا منتهب ولا مختلس قطع ) ، أخرجه التِّرْمِذِيُّ وحسنه وابن ماجه والنسائي وأبو داود وابن حبان في «صحيحه»، والطبراني في معجمه الأوسط»، والطحاوي في «شرح معاني الآثار». فان قلت : قد روى مسلم عن عائشة رضي الله عنها : «إن امرأة مخزومية كانت تستعير المتاع وتجحده فأمر النبي بقطع يدها ) ، وهذا يدل على ثبوتِ القطع في الخيانة، وبه قال أحمد . قلت : قد ثبت في رواية أخرى لمسلم وروايات أصحاب السنن والبخاري أن تلك المرأة كانت سرقت فلذلك قطع يدها لا لمجرد الخيانة في الأمانة.
[٤] قوله : وخَلس ونهب ; الفرق بينهما : أن قيد السرعة معتبر في الأول دون الثاني ، وقيد القهر معتبر في الثاني دون الأول، فأنهم فسّروا الخلس : وهو – بفتح الخاء المعجمة، وسكون اللام ، آخره سين مهمله - : بأخذ شيء جهراً سرعة، وفسروا النهب : – بفتح النون ، وسكون الهاء ، آخره باء موحدة – : بأخذ شيء علانية بقهر .
And not for a dog, and a leopard, and breach of trust, and snatching, and plunder. [1] His statement: 'property' because the purpose of taking it is to take property, so whoever writes his name in it for example. [2] His statement: 'and not for a dog and leopard' meaning no cutting for stealing a dog and leopard owned by a man; because the species of these two animals is found permissible in the abode of Islam, so it enters into insignificant (tafih). Also, they differed regarding the property status of the dog. According to al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Malik in one narration, it has no property status; therefore they did not permit its sale. According to Abu Hanifa and his two companions and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, it is permissible to sell dogs that are beneficial, and that is evidence of property status. This difference created a doubt (shubha) in property status, so the hadd penalty is dropped, as in al-Binaya. [3] His statement: 'and breach of trust' meaning no cutting for breach of trust: that the trustee betrays the trust regarding the property of the depositor in his possession. For breach of trust is not theft, just as plundering and filching are not included in theft, so there is no cutting for them either, due to the hadith: 'There is no cutting for a betrayer, nor for a plunderer, nor for a filcher.' It was narrated by al-Tirmidhi and he declared it hasan, and by Ibn Majah, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Ibn Hibban in his Sahih, al-Tabarani in his al-Mu'jam al-Awsat, and al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar. If you object: Muslim narrated from 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) that a Makhzumi woman used to borrow goods and deny them, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered her hand to be cut, and this indicates the establishment of cutting for breach of trust, and Ahmad held this view. I reply: It has been established in another narration of Muslim and narrations of the authors of Sunan and al-Bukhari that that woman had stolen, so her hand was cut for that, not merely for breach of trust. [4] His statement: 'and snatching and plunder' — the difference between them is that the condition of speed is considered in the first but not the second, and the condition of force is considered in the second but not the first. They interpreted 'snatching' (khalas) — with fatha on the kha', sukun on the lam, ending with sin — as taking something openly quickly. And they interpreted 'plunder' (nahb) — with fatha on the nun, sukun on the ha', ending with ba — as taking something openly with force.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is part of the chapter on theft, listing items and actions for which hand cutting is not applied. The first item is dogs and leopards: even if owned, cutting is not applied because these animals are usually permissible (mubah) in the land of Islam, making them considered insignificant (tafih). Furthermore, there is ikhtilaf on whether dogs have property value (maliyyah). The Shafi'i, Hanbali, and Maliki schools in one view say no property value, so they prohibit sale; the Hanafis say dogs with benefit have property value, so sale is allowed. This difference creates a doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd. Next is breach of trust (khiyanah): the depositor's trustee taking property from the trust is not theft because the property was already in his possession, so no cutting. A hadith explicitly says no cutting for traitor, plunderer, or filcher. An objection is raised using a hadith about a Makhzumi woman who borrowed and denied, and her hand was cut. The response is that she actually stole, not merely breach of trust. Finally, snatching (khalas) and plunder (nahb) are defined: snatching is taking openly but quickly, while plunder is taking openly with force. Both are distinguished from theft which requires stealth, so no cutting.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyanah): Breach of trust; when a trustee takes property entrusted to him without permission, but it is not considered theft because the property is already in his possession.
خلس (khalas): Snatching; taking something openly but quickly, without force.
نهب (nahb): Plunder; taking something openly with force (qahr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary reason that hand cutting is not applied for stealing a dog?
A) Dogs are never considered property.
B) The hadd is only for items worth more than ten dirhams.
C) The dog's species is mubah in Dar al-Islam, and the ikhtilaf on its property status creates a shubha.
D) The Prophet prohibited cutting for any animal theft.

Key Takeaway
The hadd of theft is not applied for stealing a dog or leopard due to doubt (shubha) from their mubah origin and scholarly disagreement on property status.

No Cutting for Breach of Trust (Khiyanah)
Explains that breach of trust does not incur hand cutting and provides hadith evidence, addressing a potential objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish breach of trust from theft and apply the hadith 'La qat' ala kha'in' to justify no cutting.

Core Idea
Breach of trust is not theft; the hadd is specific to stealthy taking from custody, not to betrayal of trust.

Theft (sariqa) requires taking property from a place of custody (hirz) by stealth. Breach of trust (khiyanah) occurs when a trustee, who already has lawful possession, takes property without permission. This distinction is crucial because the severe punishment of hand cutting is reserved for sariqa, not for khiyanah. The lesson also addresses an objection from a well-known hadith about a Makhzumi woman.

Guided Path
1
Define khiyanah
Khiyanah is when a depositary (mudi') or trustee takes property entrusted to him without the owner's consent. The property is already in his hand lawfully.
This contrasts with theft where the property is taken from a place of custody.
2
State the ruling and its evidence
The text rules no cutting (la qat') for khiyanah. The evidence is the hadith: 'There is no cutting for a betrayer, nor for a plunderer, nor for a filcher.' (Tirmidhi, etc.)
The hadith explicitly excludes these categories from the hadd.
3
Analyze the objection from the Makhzumi woman hadith
A hadith from Muslim reports that a woman used to borrow goods and deny them, and the Prophet cut her hand. This seems to indicate cutting for khiyanah. Ahmad b. Hanbal held this view.
The Hanafis respond by clarifying that she actually stole; other narrations confirm she stole, not just borrowed and denied.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response shows that the hadith about the Makhzumi woman is not about simple khiyanah but about theft. Thus it does not contradict the general rule.
This demonstrates the importance of examining all narrations and the context.

Next is breach of trust (khiyanah): the depositor's trustee taking property from the trust is not theft because the property was already in his possession, so no cutting. A hadith explicitly says no cutting for traitor, plunderer, or filcher. An objection is raised using a hadith about a Makhzumi woman who borrowed and denied, and her hand was cut. The response is that she actually stole, not merely breach of trust. Finally, snatching (khalas) and plunder (nahb) are defined: snatching is taking openly but quickly, while plunder is taking openly with force. Both are distinguished from theft which requires stealth, so no cutting.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyanah): Breach of trust; when a trustee takes property entrusted to him without permission, but it is not considered theft because the property is already in his possession.
خلس (khalas): Snatching; taking something openly but quickly, without force.
نهب (nahb): Plunder; taking something openly with force (qahr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence that the Hanafi school uses to justify no cutting for breach of trust?
A) The Quranic verse on theft.
B) The hadith 'La qat' ala kha'in'.
C) Analogy from dog theft.
D) Consensus of the Sahaba.

Key Takeaway
Breach of trust (khiyanah) is not subject to hadd because the property is already in the trustee's possession, and an explicit hadith excludes betrayers from cutting.

No Cutting for Snatching (Khalas) and Plunder (Nahb)
Defines snatching and plunder and confirms no cutting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate snatching (khalas) and plunder (nahb) from theft (sariqa) and state the ruling of no hand cutting.

Core Idea
Theft requires stealth; open taking, whether quickly or with force, does not meet the definition of sariqa.

The hadd of theft is specifically for sariqa, which involves taking property from a place of custody with stealth. Snatching (khalas) and plunder (nahb) are both open forms of taking, differing in speed and force. Because they lack the element of stealth, they are not classified as theft and do not incur hand cutting. This lesson also defines these terms clearly.

Guided Path
1
Define snatching (khalas) and plunder (nahb)
Khalas: taking something openly but quickly, without force. Nahb: taking something openly with force (qahr). Both are public, not stealthy.
The key difference is speed vs. force.
2
State the ruling: no cutting
The text explicitly says 'la qat' ' (no cutting) for khalas and nahb, citing the same hadith that applies to khiyanah.
The hadith covers all three: betrayer, plunderer, snatcher.
3
Explain why they are not theft
Theft (sariqa) requires ikhfa' (stealth) and taking from hirz (custody). Khalas and nahb are open acts; there is no stealth.
This is a key conceptual distinction in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Note the practical implications
Although not subject to hadd, these acts are still sinful and subject to ta'zir (discretionary punishment) and civil liability.
The severity of hadd is reserved for stealthy theft.

[1] His statement: 'property' because the purpose of taking it is to take property, so whoever writes his name in it for example. [2] His statement: 'and not for a dog and leopard' meaning no cutting for stealing a dog and leopard owned by a man; because the species of these two animals is found permissible in the abode of Islam, so it enters into insignificant (tafih). This passage is part of the chapter on theft, listing items and actions for which hand cutting is not applied. Both are distinguished from theft which requires stealth, so no cutting.

خلس (khalas): Snatching; taking something openly but quickly, without force.
نهب (nahb): Plunder; taking something openly with force (qahr).

Exemptions from Amputation for Theft: Dogs, Falcons, Breach of Trust, Snatching, Plunder
Ruling that amputation is not applied for these acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on amputation for these five categories and explain the legal reasoning—including the role of shubha and the necessity of secrecy—that exempts each from the hadd.

Core Idea
Amputation is dropped when any condition of theft is doubtful; value disputes and lack of secrecy exempt dog/falcon theft, khiyana, khalas, and nahb.

The hadd of amputation for theft is a severe penalty, and the Shari'a has set strict conditions to prevent its misuse. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the boundaries: not every taking of another's property constitutes theft punishable by amputation. We will examine why dogs, falcons, breach of trust, snatching, and plunder are excluded, focusing on the underlying principles of value certainty, secrecy, and secure custody (hirz).

Guided Path
1
Identify the five excluded acts
List the five categories: theft of dogs and falcons, breach of trust (khiyana), snatching (khalas), and plunder (nahb).
These are the acts for which amputation is not applied according to the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the reasons for exclusion
For dogs and falcons: the value (maliyya) is disputed among scholars, creating a shubha (doubt) that drops the hadd. For khiyana, khalas, and nahb: they lack the element of secret taking from a secure place (hirz).
The hadd requires both the property to be valuable (mal mutaqawwim) and the taking to be secretly from hirz.
3
Analyze the primary proof
The hadith 'Laysa 'ala kha'in wa la muntahib wa la mukhtalis qat'' directly excludes these three categories from amputation. Identify its chain and textual indication.
The hadith is ahad but has been accepted by Hanafi jurists; its explicit negation is clear.
4
Address the counter-evidence
The story of the Makhzumi woman (who borrowed and denied) might suggest amputation for khiyana. Explain that other narrations confirm she had actually stolen, so the hadd was for theft, not breach of trust.
This reconciles the apparent conflict and reinforces the principle that khiyana alone does not warrant amputation.

[2] His saying: 'nor in a dog or falcon'; meaning amputation is not applied for stealing a owned dog or falcon of a man; because the species of these two animals exists permissible in Dar al-Islam, so it enters into the trivial. For they interpreted 'snatching' – it is with fat-ha on the khā' and sukun on the lām, ending with a sīn – as taking something openly and quickly. And they interpreted 'plunder' – and its weight is the same as snatching – as taking something openly by force. Both are not theft because there is no secret taking in them, just as breach of trust is not theft because there is no taking from a secure place in it.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the rulings of theft (sariqa) regarding what does not necessitate amputation. It begins by explaining that the object of theft must be wealth (mal); writing one's name in a register is not theft because the purpose is not the paper but the record. Then it covers four categories: dogs, falcons, breach of trust, snatching, and plunder. For dogs and falcons, the ruling is that amputation is not applied even if owned, because these animals are found wild in Islamic lands and thus considered trivial (tafih). Additionally, there is a scholarly disagreement on the property status (maliyya) of dogs: the Shafi'is, Hanbalis, and Malikis in one view deny its property value, while Abu Hanifa, his two companions, and Ibrahim al-Nakha'i allow selling beneficial dogs, proving they have value. This disagreement creates a doubt (shubha) in value, which drops the hadd penalty. For breach of trust (khiyana), amputation is not applied because it lacks the element of secret taking from a secure place (hirz). The same applies to snatching (khalas) and plunder (nahb). Snatching is taking openly and quickly, while plunder is taking openly by force; neither involves secrecy. The commentary addresses a potential objection: a hadith by Muslim mentions a woman who borrowed and denied, yet the Prophet cut off her hand. The commentator clarifies that other narratives show she actually stole, so the amputation was for theft, not merely breach of trust. This passage thus reinforces the strict conditions for the hadd of theft: the taking must be secretly from a secure place, and the object must be valuable (mal mutaqawwim).

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyānah): Breach of trust: a depositary taking the deposited property without permission.
خلس (khalṣ): Snatching: taking something openly and quickly.
نهب (nahb): Plunder: taking something openly by force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because a dog is never considered property in any school.
B) Because the disagreement among scholars on its property status creates doubt (shubha) that drops the hadd.
C) Because dogs are not kept in secure places (hirz).
D) Because the hadith of the Makhzumi woman shows leniency.

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft is only applied when all conditions are met without doubt; any shubha in value or lack of secrecy drops the hadd.
