Grave Robbing and the Hadd of Theft
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on amputation for grave robbing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on amputation for grave robbing and distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad from Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i by citing the key proofs and legal reasoning.

Core Idea
No amputation for grave robbing because the grave is not a secure place (hirz) and ownership is doubtful.

In this lesson, we examine a classical intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute: does a grave robber face the hadd of amputation for theft? The passage from *Umda al-Ri'aya* presents the Hanafi position (no amputation) alongside the objections of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i. By working through the proofs, you will understand why the madhhab prioritizes doubt over the literal application of the hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Define grave robbing (nabsh) and state the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: no amputation.
The ruling applies whether the grave is locked or in the desert, and whether the item is the shroud or something else.
2
Examine the Hanafi Proof (Dalil)
Locate the two reasons: lack of hirz (the grave is not a secure place) and doubt in ownership (shubha). Note the supporting narrations from Ibn Abbas and the practice of Marwan with Companion consensus.
Hirz is a condition for sariqa; the deceased cannot guard, and the grave is not a storage place for the living.
3
State the Opposing Position and Its Proof
Abu Yusuf (within Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i hold amputation is wajib. Their proofs: precedents of Umar and Ibn al-Zubayr, and a hadith 'Whoever robs a grave, we will amputate him.'
The hadith is narrated by al-Bayhaqi as marfu'; the author of al-Hidayah denied its authenticity, but the commentator dismisses that denial.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response: the hadith's chain is disputed, the Companions' actions are not unanimous, and the lack of hirz and ownership doubt remain decisive.
Marwan's practice with many Companions present is cited as the stronger precedent.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
In the mufta bihi position, a grave robber receives ta'zir (discretionary punishment) but not amputation. Under Abu Yusuf and Shafi'i, amputation would apply.
This difference affects actual sentencing in a Hanafi jurisdiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1]قوله : ونبش ; النبش – بالفتح : أخذ كفن الميت بعد الدفن، وعدم القطع في النبش مذهب أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله سواء كان القبر في بيت مقفل أو في مفازة، وسواء كان المأخوذ من القبر هو كفن الميت أو غيره. والوجه في ذلك : أن النبش ليس بسرق لفوات الحرز، فإن الميت لا يحرز نفسه والقبر ليس بمحل للحرز; ولأن الشبهة تمكنت في الملك ; إذ لا ملك للميت حقيقةً ولا للوارث لتقدم حاجة الميت من التجهيز والتكفين على حق الورثة. كذا في حواشي «الهداية». ويؤيده قول ابن عباس : ليس على النباش قطع ، أخرجه ابن أبي شَيْبَةَ ، وأخرج أيضاً عن الزُّهْرِيُّ الله قال : «أُتي مروان بقوم يختفون – أي ينبشون – القبور فضربهم ونفاهم والصحابة متوافرون». وأخرجه عبد الرزاق، وزاد: «وطوف بهم». وفي رواية لابن أبي شيبة عن الزهري قال : «أُخذ نباش في زمان معاوية ، وكان مروان على المدينة، فسأل من يحضر من الصحابة والفقهاء، فأجمع رأيهم على أن يضرب ويطاف به». وذهب أبو يوسف والشافعي الله إلى وجوب قطع النباش وسلفهما في ذلك ابن الزبير، فإنه قطع نباشاً، أخرجه البخاري في «التاريخ»، وثبت مثله عن عمر ، أخرجه عبد الرزاق أيضاً، ويوافقهما حديث: «من نبش قطعناه»(۳)، أخرجه البَيْهَقِيُّ مرفوعاً، وإنكارُ صاحب «الهداية) عن كونه مرفوعاً ليس بشيء، كما أن ذكره الحديث
[1] His saying: 'and grave robbing'; grave robbing (al-nabsh) – with fat-ha: taking the shroud of the deceased after burial. The absence of amputation in grave robbing is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, whether the grave is in a locked house or in the desert, and whether what is taken from the grave is the shroud of the deceased or something else. The reasoning for that: that grave robbing is not theft because the element of secure place (hirz) is lacking, for the deceased does not guard himself and the grave is not a place for security; and because doubt (shubha) is established in ownership, since the deceased does not truly own and the heir does not own because the deceased's need for preparation and shrouding takes precedence over the rights of the heirs. Thus in the glosses of al-Hidayah. This is supported by the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'There is no amputation for the grave robber,' narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah, and also from al-Zuhri, may Allah have mercy on him, who said: 'Marwan was brought a group who were digging up graves – i.e., robbing graves – so he beat them and banished them, while the Companions were abundant.' And Abd al-Razzaq narrated it with the addition: 'and paraded them.' In a narration of Ibn Abi Shaybah from al-Zuhri: 'A grave robber was caught in the time of Muawiya, and Marwan was in Medina, so he asked the present Companions and jurists, and they all agreed that he should be beaten and paraded.' Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, went to the obligation of amputation for the grave robber, and their predecessor in that was Ibn al-Zubayr, for he amputated a grave robber, narrated by al-Bukhari in al-Tarikh, and similar is established from Umar, narrated by Abd al-Razzaq as well, and supports them the hadith: 'Whoever robs a grave, we will amputate him,' narrated by al-Bayhaqi as marfu'. The denier of al-Hidayah's author that it is marfu' is nothing, as is his mention of the hadith.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage discusses grave robbing (nabsh), defined as taking the shroud of a deceased person after burial. The Hanafi school, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, holds that amputation is not applied for grave robbing, whether the grave is in a locked building or an open desert, and regardless of whether the taken item is the shroud or something else. The reasoning is twofold: first, the condition of a secure place (hirz) is not met because the deceased cannot guard property and a grave is not a secure location; second, there is a doubt (shubha) about ownership—the deceased does not own the shroud after death, and the heirs' ownership is contingent on the deceased's needs being fulfilled, so no clear-cut ownership exists. This view is supported by the statement of Ibn Abbas that there is no amputation for a grave robber, and by reports that Marwan ibn al-Hakam, with the presence of many Companions, punished grave robbers with beating and parading but not amputation. On the other hand, Abu Yusuf (a leading Hanafi authority) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that amputation is obligatory for grave robbing. They cite the precedent of Ibn al-Zubayr and Umar ibn al-Khattab who amputated grave robbers, and a hadith attributed to the Prophet: 'Whoever robs a grave, we will amputate him.' The commentator notes that the author of al-Hidayah's denial of the hadith being marfu' (elevated to the Prophet) is not valid. Thus, this is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side and Abu Yusuf on the other, as well as an inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i. The operative position in the Hanafi school is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (no amputation), which is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Key Terms
نبش (nabsh): Grave robbing: taking the shroud or other items from a deceased person after burial.
حرز (ḥirz): A secure place where property is kept; necessary for theft to be sariqa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad for not amputating a grave robber?
A) The grave robber is poor and thus exempt.
B) The shroud is not considered property after burial.
C) The grave is not a secure place (hirz) and there is doubt in ownership.
D) The deceased does not need the shroud anymore.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd of amputation for theft is waived for grave robbing due to the absence of a secure place (hirz) and the presence of ownership doubt (shubha).

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
beginner

Graverobbing (Nabsh) and Hand-Cutting: The Hanafi Position
Dispute on whether graverobbing constitutes theft warranting hand-cutting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on hand-cutting for graverobbing and explain the key arguments (Hirz, Shubha) that distinguish it from theft.

Core Idea
A grave is not a secure place (Hirz) and ownership is doubtful — so no hand-cutting for the grave robber.

This passage addresses a classical dispute: should a grave robber (Nabhash) receive the hadd punishment of hand-cutting? The Hanafi school, represented by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, says no, while Abu Yusuf (within the school) and al-Shafi'i say yes. Understanding the reasoning reveals how Hanafi jurists apply conditions like Hirz and the principle of Shubha to restrict hudud punishments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is Nabsh (graverobbing) – taking the shroud from a grave after burial. The Hanafi ruling is that the hand of the grave robber is not cut.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and it is the mufta bihi in the school.
2
Understand the two main reasons
First, the lack of Hirz (secure place): the grave is not considered a secure place for property. Second, the presence of Shubha (doubt) in ownership: the deceased does not own the shroud in a way that triggers the hadd, and the heirs' ownership is subordinate to the deceased's immediate needs.
These reasons are based on the conditions for the theft hadd: property taken from a Hirz and clear ownership.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The text cites the saying of Ibn Abbas ('No cutting for the grave robber'), the practice of Marwan under the Caliphs, and a consensus among Companions on beating and parading, not cutting.
This is the Hanafi dalil.
4
Contrast with the opposing position
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue for cutting based on a marfu' hadith ('Whoever robs a grave, we cut him') and precedents from Ibn al-Zubayr and Umar.
The Hanafi response is that the hadith is weak in transmission (majhul narrator) and is interpreted as a policy punishment (Siyasah), not a fixed hadd.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: in Hanafi fiqh, the grave robber is not subject to hand-cutting but may receive ta'zir. In Shafi'i fiqh, he is subject to hand-cutting.
This shows the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text

النَّهْب : ووزنُهُ وزنُ الخَلْس : أخذ الشيء على وجه العلانية قهراً. وكل منهما ليس بسرقة ؛ لعدم وجود الأخذ خفية فيهما كما أن الخيانة ليست بسرقة لعدم وجود الأخذ من الحرز فيها.
→
[1]قوله : ونبش ؛ النبش – بالفتح : أخذ كفن الميت بعد الدفن، وعدم القطع في النبش مذهب أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله سواء كان القبر في بيت مقفل أو في مفازة، وسواء كان المأخوذ من القبر هو كفن الميت أو غيره.
والوجه في ذلك : أن النبش ليس بسرق لفوات الحرز، فإن الميت لا يحرز نفسه والقبر ليس بمحل للحرز؛ ولأن الشبهة تمكنت في الملك ؛ إذ لا ملك للميت حقيقةً ولا للوارث لتقدم حاجة الميت من التجهيز والتكفين على حق الورثة. كذا في حواشي «الهداية».
ويؤيده قول ابن عباس : ليس على النباش قطع ، أخرجه ابن أبي شَيْبَةَ ، وأخرج أيضاً عن الزُّهْرِيُّ الله قال : «أُتي مروان بقوم يختفون – أي ينبشون – القبور فضربهم ونفاهم والصحابة متوافرون». وأخرجه عبد الرزاق، وزاد: «وطوف بهم»
وفي رواية لابن أبي شيبة عن الزهري قال : «أُخذ نباش في زمان معاوية ، وكان مروان على المدينة، فسأل من يحضر من الصحابة والفقهاء، فأجمع رأيهم على أن يضرب ويطاف به».
وذهب أبو يوسف والشافعي الله إلى وجوب قطع النباش وسلفهما في ذلك ابن ق الزبير، فإنه قطع نباشاً، أخرجه البخاري في «التاريخ»، وثبت مثله عن عمر ، أخرجه عبد الرزاق أيضاً، ويوافقهما حديث: «من نبش قطعناه»(۳)، أخرجه البَيْهَقِيُّ مرفوعاً، وإنكارُ صاحب «الهداية) عن كونه مرفوعاً ليس بشيء، كما أن ذكره الحديث المرفوع بلفظ : «لا قطع على المختفي» لا يعبأ به.
والجواب من قبل أبي حنيفة الله عن حديث : «من نبش قطعناه» :
أولاً : بأن في سنده من يجهل حاله فلا يعتبر به.
وثانيا : بأنه محمول على السياسة، ويؤيده أنه ورد في بعض الروايات مع تلك الجملة : ومَن غَرّق غرّقناه» (١) ، وبالجملة ليس في الباب حديث مرفوع يروي بسند يحتج به يدل على قطع النباش أو عدم قطعه، واختلاف الصحابة له فيه مبني على أن النبش هل هو سرقة أم لا؟ فمَن ظن أنه سرقة أفتى بالقطع ، ومَن ظنَّ أنه ليس بسرقة لاختلال الحرز والملكِ أفتى بعدم القطع إلا سياسة، ولعل الحقَّ يدور حوله ما لم يثبت عن صاحب الشريعة بسند يحتج به خلافه.
ويرد في هذا المقام أن الطّرَّ، وهو أخذ مال الغير، وهو يقظان حاضر قاصد لحفظه بضرب غفلة منه أيضاً ليس بسرقة، كما أن النبش ليس بسرقة، فما بالهم حكموا بقطع يد الطرار دون النباش؟ والجواب عنه : على ما بسطه ابنُ ملك في «شرح المنار» وغيره من الأصوليين : إن آية السرقة التي فيها حكم القطع ظاهرة فيما وضع له السارق خفية في حق الطرار والنباش ، فنظرنا في أن الخفاء فيهما لمزيةٍ أو نقصان، فظهر لنا أن الخفاء في حق الطرار لزيادة، فإن الطرّار وإن كان غير السرقة لكن فيه زيادة على السرقة، فإنَّ السارق يأخذ خفية، والطرّار يأخذ علانية بنوع غفلة.
وإن الخفاء في حق النباش ؛ لنقصان ؛ لأنّ النبش أدون من السرقة، فأوجبنا القطع بالطر دون النبش بناءً على أن الحكم إذا ثبت للأدنى ثبت للأعلى بالطريق الأولى ولا عكس.
(The text discusses Nabsh) Plundering (Nahb): its measure is the measure of snatching (Khals): taking something openly by force. Both of these are not theft because there is no secret taking in them, just as betrayal (Khiyanah) is not theft because there is no taking from a secure place (Hirz). (Says the commentator: His statement: 'and Nabsh') Nabsh – with a fatha: taking the shroud of the deceased after burial. The absence of hand-cutting in Nabsh is the doctrine of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, whether the grave is in a locked house or in the desert, and whether what is taken from the grave is the shroud of the deceased or something else. The reasoning for that is that Nabsh is not theft because Hirz is absent, for the deceased does not secure himself and the grave is not a place of Hirz; and because doubt (Shubha) is present in ownership, since the deceased has no actual ownership nor does the heir, because the deceased's need for preparation and shrouding precedes the right of the heirs. Thus it is in the glosses of al-Hidayah. And it is supported by the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'There is no cutting for the grave robber,' reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah, and also reported from al-Zuhri who said: 'Marwan was brought some people who were robbing (i.e., digging) graves, so he beat them and exiled them while the Companions were many.' And Abd al-Razzaq reported it and added: 'And he paraded them.' In another narration from Ibn Abi Shaybah from al-Zuhri: 'A grave robber was caught in the time of Muawiyah, and Marwan was over Medina, so he asked the attending Companions and jurists, and they all agreed that he should be beaten and paraded.' Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i went to the obligation of cutting the grave robber. Their predecessor in that is Ibn al-Zubayr, for he cut a grave robber, reported by al-Bukhari in al-Tarikh, and similar is established from Umar, reported by Abd al-Razzaq as well. And it is supported by the hadith: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him,' reported by al-Bayhaqi as marfu' (raised to the Prophet). The denial of the author of al-Hidayah that it is marfu' is nothing, just as his mention of the marfu' hadith with the wording: 'No cutting for the secret (taker)' is not to be regarded. The response from Abu Hanifa to the hadith 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him': First, that in its chain there is someone whose condition is unknown, so it is not considered. Second, that it is interpreted as for policy (Siyasah), supported by the fact that in some narrations with that sentence: 'And whoever drowns, we drown him.' In short, there is no marfu' hadith in this chapter reported with a chain that can be used as evidence indicating cutting the grave robber or not. The disagreement of the Companions on it is based on whether Nabsh is theft or not? So whoever thought it is theft gave a fatwa of cutting, and whoever thought it is not theft due to the defect in Hirz and ownership gave a fatwa of no cutting except as policy. Perhaps the truth revolves around this until the contrary is established from the Lawgiver with an acceptable chain. And at this point it may be objected: the pickpocket (Tarrar), who takes another's property while the owner is awake and present intending to guard it, through a type of heedlessness, is also not theft, just as Nabsh is not theft. So why did they rule cutting the hand of the pickpocket but not the grave robber? The answer to this, as elaborated by Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Manar and other usul scholars: The verse of theft, which contains the ruling of cutting, is apparent regarding what a thief is defined as (secret taking) in the case of the pickpocket and the grave robber. So we considered whether the secrecy in them is due to an advantage or a deficiency. It became clear to us that secrecy in the case of the pickpocket is due to an increase (i.e., he is more cunning), for the pickpocket, though not a thief, has an increase over theft: the thief takes secretly, while the pickpocket takes openly with a kind of heedlessness. And secrecy in the case of the grave robber is due to a deficiency, because grave robbing is lower than theft. So we imposed cutting for pickpocketing but not for grave robbing, based on the principle that if a ruling is established for the lower, it is established for the higher by primary inference, and not vice versa.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling on graverobbing (Nabsh) and whether it entails hand-cutting (hadd al-sariqa). The author begins by defining related terms: Nahb (plundering) and Khals (snatching) are not theft because they lack secrecy. The core issue is Nabsh: taking a shroud from a grave after burial. The position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani is that there is no cutting for the grave robber (Nabhash). They argue that the element of Hirz (secure place) is absent because the deceased does not secure himself and a grave is not considered a secure place. Additionally, there is doubt (Shubha) regarding ownership: the deceased has no actual ownership, and the heirs' right is subordinate to the deceased's need for shrouding. This is supported by the saying of Ibn Abbas and the practice of Marwan and the Companions, who punished grave robbers with beating and exile, not cutting. On the other hand, Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that the grave robber should be cut. They cite the precedent of Ibn al-Zubayr and Umar, and a marfu' hadith: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him.' The author of al-Hidayah questioned the authenticity of this hadith, but the commentator here dismisses that objection. Abu Hanifa's response to the hadith is twofold: first, its chain has an unknown narrator; second, it is to be interpreted as a discretionary policy punishment (Siyasah), not a fixed hadd. The passage then addresses a subtle objection: why is the pickpocket (Tarrar) cut but not the grave robber, since both are not technically theft? The answer is based on Usul al-Fiqh: the verse of theft (Quran 5:38) is understood as applying to secret taking. In pickpocketing, the secrecy is due to a heightened cunning (Ziyadah), making it more severe than ordinary theft, so cutting is applied by primary inference (al-tariq al-awla). In graverobbing, the secrecy is due to a deficiency (Nuqsan) — the act is lower in gravity — so cutting is not applied. Thus, the ruling for the higher (pickpocketing) includes the lower (theft), but the lower (graverobbing) does not include the higher.

Key Terms
النَّبْش (an-nabsh): Taking the shroud of a deceased person from the grave after burial.
الطَّرَّار (at-tarrār): A pickpocket who takes another's property openly through a type of heedlessness, while the owner is awake and aware.
الحرز (al-ḥirz): A secure place where property is protected; a condition for the application of the hadd of theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why is there no hand-cutting for the grave robber (Nabhash)?
A) Because the grave is not a secure place (Hirz) and there is ownership doubt.
B) Because the grave robber is not a thief by definition.
C) Because the shroud belongs to the heirs, not the deceased.
D) Because the hadith prohibits cutting in such cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not apply hand-cutting to graverobbing because the grave is not a Hirz and ownership is doubtful (Shubha); only ta'zir applies.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate

Pickpocketing vs Graverobbing: The Logic of Primary Inference (al-Tariq al-Awla)
Explanation of the usul-based distinction between pickpocketing and graverobbing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of primary inference (al-tariq al-awla) to explain why a pickpocket is cut but a grave robber is not.

Core Idea
If a ruling applies to a lesser act, it applies to a greater act by primary inference; but not vice versa.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if both pickpocketing (Tarrar) and graverobbing (Nabsh) are not technically theft, why is the pickpocket subject to hand-cutting while the grave robber is not? The Hanafi answer is a masterclass in usul al-fiqh: the element of secrecy (khafa) in pickpocketing is due to increased cunning, making it more severe than theft, while in graverobbing it is due to deficiency. Hence, cutting for pickpocketing is established by primary inference (al-tariq al-awla), but cutting for graverobbing is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says: both pickpocketing (Tarrar) and graverobbing (Nabsh) are not theft because they lack the conditions of Hirz and secrecy, yet the school cuts the pickpocket but not the grave robber. Why the inconsistency?
This objection is raised in the text itself.
2
Understand the Hanafi classification of secrecy
Hanafi jurists analyze the nature of secrecy (khafa) in each act. In theft, secrecy is present (taking stealthily). In pickpocketing, the taking is open but with a form of heedlessness; this secrecy is due to increased cunning (ziyadah). In graverobbing, the taking is also open but due to the deficiency of the act (nuqsan).
The key is the quality of the secrecy, not its mere presence.
3
Apply the principle of primary inference (al-tariq al-awla)
The rule: if a ruling is established for a lower case (theft), it is established for a higher case (pickpocketing) by primary inference (al-tariq al-awla). But the reverse does not apply: just because the lower case (theft) is cut does not mean the even lower case (graverobbing) is cut, because graverobbing is deficient compared to theft.
This is a qiyas awlawi (a fortiori analogy).
4
Practical outcome
The pickpocket is cut because his act is more severe than theft; the grave robber is not cut because his act is less severe than theft.
Thus, the ruling is consistent within the Hanafi framework.

وثانيا : بأنه محمول على السياسة، ويؤيده أنه ورد في بعض الروايات مع تلك الجملة : ومَن غَرّق غرّقناه» (١) ، وبالجملة ليس في الباب حديث مرفوع يروي بسند يحتج به يدل على قطع النباش أو عدم قطعه، واختلاف الصحابة له فيه مبني على أن النبش هل هو سرقة أم لا؟ فمَن ظن أنه سرقة أفتى بالقطع ، ومَن ظنَّ أنه ليس بسرقة لاختلال الحرز والملكِ أفتى بعدم القطع إلا سياسة، ولعل الحقَّ يدور حوله ما لم يثبت عن صاحب الشريعة بسند يحتج به خلافه.
ويرد في هذا المقام أن الطّرَّ، وهو أخذ مال الغير، وهو يقظان حاضر قاصد لحفظه بضرب غفلة منه أيضاً ليس بسرقة، كما أن النبش ليس بسرقة، فما بالهم حكموا بقطع يد الطرار دون النباش؟ والجواب عنه : على ما بسطه ابنُ ملك في «شرح المنار» وغيره من الأصوليين : إن آية السرقة التي فيها حكم القطع ظاهرة فيما وضع له السارق خفية في حق الطرار والنباش ، فنظرنا في أن الخفاء فيهما لمزيةٍ أو نقصان، فظهر لنا أن الخفاء في حق الطرار لزيادة، فإن الطرّار وإن كان غير السرقة لكن فيه زيادة على السرقة، فإنَّ السارق يأخذ خفية، والطرّار يأخذ علانية بنوع غفلة.
وإن الخفاء في حق النباش ؛ لنقصان ؛ لأنّ النبش أدون من السرقة، فأوجبنا القطع بالطر دون النبش بناءً على أن الحكم إذا ثبت للأدنى ثبت للأعلى بالطريق الأولى ولا عكس.
(The text discusses Nabsh) Plundering (Nahb): its measure is the measure of snatching (Khals): taking something openly by force. Both of these are not theft because there is no secret taking in them, just as betrayal (Khiyanah) is not theft because there is no taking from a secure place (Hirz). (Says the commentator: His statement: 'and Nabsh') Nabsh – with a fatha: taking the shroud of the deceased after burial. The absence of hand-cutting in Nabsh is the doctrine of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, whether the grave is in a locked house or in the desert, and whether what is taken from the grave is the shroud of the deceased or something else. The reasoning for that is that Nabsh is not theft because Hirz is absent, for the deceased does not secure himself and the grave is not a place of Hirz; and because doubt (Shubha) is present in ownership, since the deceased has no actual ownership nor does the heir, because the deceased's need for preparation and shrouding precedes the right of the heirs. Thus it is in the glosses of al-Hidayah. And it is supported by the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'There is no cutting for the grave robber,' reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah, and also reported from al-Zuhri who said: 'Marwan was brought some people who were robbing (i.e., digging) graves, so he beat them and exiled them while the Companions were many.' And Abd al-Razzaq reported it and added: 'And he paraded them.' In another narration from Ibn Abi Shaybah from al-Zuhri: 'A grave robber was caught in the time of Muawiyah, and Marwan was over Medina, so he asked the attending Companions and jurists, and they all agreed that he should be beaten and paraded.' Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i went to the obligation of cutting the grave robber. Their predecessor in that is Ibn al-Zubayr, for he cut a grave robber, reported by al-Bukhari in al-Tarikh, and similar is established from Umar, reported by Abd al-Razzaq as well. And it is supported by the hadith: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him,' reported by al-Bayhaqi as marfu' (raised to the Prophet). The denial of the author of al-Hidayah that it is marfu' is nothing, just as his mention of the marfu' hadith with the wording: 'No cutting for the secret (taker)' is not to be regarded. The response from Abu Hanifa to the hadith 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him': First, that in its chain there is someone whose condition is unknown, so it is not considered. Second, that it is interpreted as for policy (Siyasah), supported by the fact that in some narrations with that sentence: 'And whoever drowns, we drown him.' In short, there is no marfu' hadith in this chapter reported with a chain that can be used as evidence indicating cutting the grave robber or not. The disagreement of the Companions on it is based on whether Nabsh is theft or not? So whoever thought it is theft gave a fatwa of cutting, and whoever thought it is not theft due to the defect in Hirz and ownership gave a fatwa of no cutting except as policy. Perhaps the truth revolves around this until the contrary is established from the Lawgiver with an acceptable chain. And at this point it may be objected: the pickpocket (Tarrar), who takes another's property while the owner is awake and present intending to guard it, through a type of heedlessness, is also not theft, just as Nabsh is not theft. So why did they rule cutting the hand of the pickpocket but not the grave robber? The answer to this, as elaborated by Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Manar and other usul scholars: The verse of theft, which contains the ruling of cutting, is apparent regarding what a thief is defined as (secret taking) in the case of the pickpocket and the grave robber. So we considered whether the secrecy in them is due to an advantage or a deficiency. It became clear to us that secrecy in the case of the pickpocket is due to an increase (i.e., he is more cunning), for the pickpocket, though not a thief, has an increase over theft: the thief takes secretly, while the pickpocket takes openly with a kind of heedlessness. And secrecy in the case of the grave robber is due to a deficiency, because grave robbing is lower than theft. So we imposed cutting for pickpocketing but not for grave robbing, based on the principle that if a ruling is established for the lower, it is established for the higher by primary inference, and not vice versa.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling on graverobbing (Nabsh) and whether it entails hand-cutting (hadd al-sariqa). The author begins by defining related terms: Nahb (plundering) and Khals (snatching) are not theft because they lack secrecy. The core issue is Nabsh: taking a shroud from a grave after burial. The position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani is that there is no cutting for the grave robber (Nabhash). They argue that the element of Hirz (secure place) is absent because the deceased does not secure himself and a grave is not considered a secure place. Additionally, there is doubt (Shubha) regarding ownership: the deceased has no actual ownership, and the heirs' right is subordinate to the deceased's need for shrouding. This is supported by the saying of Ibn Abbas and the practice of Marwan and the Companions, who punished grave robbers with beating and exile, not cutting. On the other hand, Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that the grave robber should be cut. They cite the precedent of Ibn al-Zubayr and Umar, and a marfu' hadith: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him.' The author of al-Hidayah questioned the authenticity of this hadith, but the commentator here dismisses that objection. Abu Hanifa's response to the hadith is twofold: first, its chain has an unknown narrator; second, it is to be interpreted as a discretionary policy punishment (Siyasah), not a fixed hadd. The passage then addresses a subtle objection: why is the pickpocket (Tarrar) cut but not the grave robber, since both are not technically theft? The answer is based on Usul al-Fiqh: the verse of theft (Quran 5:38) is understood as applying to secret taking. In pickpocketing, the secrecy is due to a heightened cunning (Ziyadah), making it more severe than ordinary theft, so cutting is applied by primary inference (al-tariq al-awla). In graverobbing, the secrecy is due to a deficiency (Nuqsan) — the act is lower in gravity — so cutting is not applied. Thus, the ruling for the higher (pickpocketing) includes the lower (theft), but the lower (graverobbing) does not include the higher.

Key Terms
الطَّرَّار (at-tarrār): A pickpocket who takes another's property openly through a type of heedlessness, while the owner is awake and aware.
النَّبْش (an-nabsh): Taking the shroud of a deceased person from the grave after burial.
الطريق الأولى (al-ṭarīq al-awlā): Primary inference (a fortiori analogy): if a ruling applies to a lesser case, it applies a fortiori to a greater case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Pickpocketing has secrecy due to deficiency, graverobbing due to increase.
B) Pickpocketing has secrecy due to increase, graverobbing due to deficiency.
C) Both have secrecy due to increase.
D) Both have secrecy due to deficiency.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh cuts the pickpocket by primary inference because his act is more severe than theft, but does not cut the grave robber because his act is less severe.

Grave Robber vs Pickpocket: Hanafi Ruling on Hadd
Hanafi response to narrations about cutting the hand of a grave robber and distinction from pickpocket

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between grave robbery and pickpocketing regarding the hadd amputation and evaluate the opposing proof.

Core Idea
Grave robbery lacks proper guarding and ownership, so no hadd; pickpocketing involves enhanced concealment, so hadd applies.

The hadd penalty for theft is one of the most severe punishments in Islamic law. However, not every act of taking property qualifies as theft that incurs amputation. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on two specific cases: grave robbery (al-nabbaash) and pickpocketing (al-tarrar). While both involve taking property without consent, the Hanafi school rules differently on each. The key lies in analyzing the quality of concealment (khafa') and the conditions of proper guarding (hirz) and ownership (milk). We will work through the arguments and counterarguments to understand the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling for both the grave robber and the pickpocket. Grave robber: no cutting of the hand. Pickpocket: cutting of the hand.
This is the starting point: the ruling itself.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Some Companions and later scholars hold that the grave robber's hand should be cut, based on a hadith: 'Whoever digs up a grave, cut his hand.' Also, they analogize grave robbery to theft.
This is the contrary position that the Hanafis refute.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith and the analogy. The hadith is reported with various chains. The analogy: grave robbery is taking property secretly, so it is theft.
Understand the strength of the opposing argument.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds: the hadith's chain is weak (unknown narrator), and it is interpreted as a policy punishment (siyasa), not a fixed hadd. Further, grave robbery lacks proper hirz and milk, making it not theft. For the pickpocket, although not theft in the strict sense, the concealment is due to increased skill, so by priority (awla), the hadd applies.
This is the core of the Hanafi argument.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi courts, grave robbers receive discretionary punishment (ta'zir), not hadd; pickpockets receive hadd. In other schools, grave robbers may also receive hadd.
This shows the real-world impact of the legal disagreement.

(١) في السنن الصغير (۷) : (۲۰۱) : من حرق حرقناه ومن نبش قطعناه»، وفي «سنن البيهقي الكبير (٨ : ٤٣ ) : ومن حرق حرقناه، ومن غرق غرقناه.
The narration with the wording: 'No cutting (of the hand) for the one who hides' is not to be regarded. And the response from Abu Hanifa regarding the hadith 'Whoever digs up a grave, we cut his hand': First, that in its chain is someone whose condition is unknown, so it is not considered. Second, that it is interpreted as [a ruling of] policy, supported by the fact that in some narrations alongside that sentence it says: 'And whoever drowns, we drown him.' In summary, there is no marfu' hadith transmitted with an acceptable chain indicating cutting for the grave robber or not cutting, and the disagreement of the Companions about it is based on whether grave robbery is theft or not. Whoever thought it is theft gave a fatwa of cutting, and whoever thought it is not theft due to the deficiency of guarding and ownership gave a fatwa of non-cutting except as policy. Perhaps the truth revolves around this until the contrary is established from the Lawgiver with an acceptable chain. And in this place it is objected that the pickpocket (al-tarrar) - which is taking the property of another while he is awake, present, intending to guard it, through a type of heedlessness - is also not theft, just as grave robbery is not theft. So why did they rule cutting the hand of the pickpocket but not the grave robber? The answer, as elaborated by Ibn Malik in Sharh al-Manar and other usuli scholars: The verse of theft which contains the ruling of cutting is apparently [applied] to what the thief takes secretly in the case of the pickpocket and grave robber. We then considered whether the hiddenness in them is due to an advantage or a deficiency. It became clear to us that the hiddenness in the case of the pickpocket is due to an increase: for the pickpocket, although not theft, has an increase over theft, because the thief takes secretly while the pickpocket takes openly through a type of heedlessness. And the hiddenness in the case of the grave robber is due to a deficiency, because grave robbery is inferior to theft. So we required cutting for the pickpocket but not for the grave robber, based on the principle that if a ruling is established for the lesser, it is established for the greater by way of priority, and not vice versa. (1) In al-Sunan al-Saghir (7:201): 'Whoever burns, we burn him; whoever digs up a grave, we cut his hand.' And in Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (8:43): 'Whoever burns, we burn him; whoever drowns, we drown him.'
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute and rational analysis of the ruling on cutting the hand of a grave robber (al-nabbaash) compared to a pickpocket (al-tarrar). The author first dismisses a weak hadith that says 'no cut for the one who hides' as unreliable. Then he presents Abu Hanifa's response to the hadith 'Whoever digs up a grave, we cut his hand' (which is used by some to argue for cutting). Abu Hanifa gives two points: first, the chain contains an unknown narrator; second, the hadith is to be interpreted as a discretionary policy punishment (siyasa), not a fixed hadd, supported by the parallel phrase 'whoever drowns, we drown him' which is clearly policy. The author notes that there is no authentic marfu' hadith on the matter, and the Companions' disagreement stems from whether grave robbery qualifies as theft due to issues of proper guarding (hirz) and ownership (milk). Then an objection is raised: if grave robbery is not theft, then pickpocketing is also not theft because the victim is awake and present, yet the Hanafi school cuts the hand of the pickpocket. Why the difference? The answer, drawn from usul scholars, is based on the quality of hiddenness (khafa'). The verse on theft (Quran 5:38) applies to secret taking. In pickpocketing, the hiddenness is due to the thief's skill—an increase over ordinary theft—so it qualifies for the hadd. In grave robbery, the hiddenness is due to a deficiency (the corpse is not guarded, the property is not in full ownership), so it does not qualify. The principle is: if a ruling is established for a lesser offence (pickpocketing, which is like theft in essence), it applies a fortiori to a greater offence (theft proper), but the converse does not hold.

Key Terms
النباش (al-nabbaash): One who digs up a grave to steal the burial shroud or other property of the deceased.
الطرّار (al-tarrar): Pickpocket: one who takes property from a person while the person is awake and guarding it, through a trick or heedlessness.
الخفاء (al-khafa'): Concealment or hiddenness; the quality of taking secretly.
الحرز (al-hirz): Proper guarding or secure place for property; a condition for theft to incur hadd.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership; the property stolen must be owned by someone else and not shared with the thief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the pickpocket steals a larger amount.
B) Because the pickpocket's concealment is due to increased skill, while the grave robber's concealment is due to deficiency in guarding.
C) Because the grave robber does not take from a living person.
D) Because the pickpocket is explicitly mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between grave robbery and pickpocketing based on the quality of concealment: increase vs deficiency, leading to different hadd rulings.

Properties That Prevent the Hadd for Theft: Shubha al-Milk
Listing and proving properties with shared rights that preclude the cutting penalty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of property that preclude the hadd for theft and explain how the concept of shubha (legal doubt) applies to each.

Core Idea
Shared ownership or a claim to property creates a doubt that lifts the hadd penalty.

The Hanafi school holds that certain kinds of property, when stolen, do not trigger the hadd amputation because the thief has some legal right or share in that property. This doubt, or shubha al-milk, overrides the usual punishment. In this lesson, we will examine the three categories listed in the matn and the proofs provided from the early generations.

Guided Path
1
List the three categories
Read the matn carefully: public property (mal 'amma), property where the thief has a partnership share (shirka), and property that is the like of a right owed to the thief (mithl haqqihi). Write each down.
The matn says: 'ومال عامة، ومال له فيه شركة، ومثل حقه حالاً، أو مؤجلاً'.
2
Understand why each creates a shubha
For each category, identify the element that creates a legal doubt. Public property: every Muslim has a right in it. Shared property: the thief owns a part. Like of a right: the thief has a claim to that exact property. These doubts prevent the full hadd.
The shubha is in the ownership or right, not in the act of taking.
3
Examine the proofs from the Companions and Prophet
The text quotes 'Umar, 'Ali, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) refusing to cut off hands for theft from the treasury, spoils, and khums. Extract the key idea from each narration.
For example, 'Umar said: 'No one except that he has a right in it; there is no cutting upon him.'
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
The commentary extends the ruling to waqf property and to theft of the like of a deferred debt. It also explains that 'mithliyya' includes legal similarity (e.g., dirhams and dinars are one genus). Consider a scenario: someone steals jewelry worth the same as a debt owed to him. Does the hadd apply?
Jewelry is treated as a commodity (sil'ah), so it is a different genus; hadd would apply if nisab is met.

Source Text

[1] His saying: 'And property of the public' — in the genitive — means there is no cutting for stealing property in which the general public has a right, because every person has a right in it from some aspect, and this creates a doubt (shubha) that drops the hadd. And Ibn Abi Shayba narrated: 'A man stole from the Bayt al-Mal, so Sa'd wrote to 'Umar about it, and 'Umar said: There is no one except that he has a right in it; there is no cutting upon him.' And 'Abd al-Razzaq narrated: 'Ali was brought a man who had stolen from the spoils, and he said: He has a share in it, and it is existing, so he did not cut him.' And in Sunan Ibn Majah: 'A slave stole from the khums, and the Prophet did not cut him, and said: The wealth of Allah, some of it steals from some.' (2) From here the reason for not cutting for stealing property shared between the thief and the possessor is known, and for not cutting for stealing waqf property, and for stealing the like of his right from one who owes him a right. [2] His saying: 'And the like of his right' — meaning its apparent like, even if it differs in amount and description, as when he steals more than his right or steals in a deferred debt. And likeness (mithliyya) is more general, whether actual or legal. If he has dirhams and steals dinars, or vice versa, he is not cut because the two currencies are one legal genus, contrary to commodities, which are a different genus in every respect. Jewelry is among these because due to the craftsmanship it is attached to commodities. As stated in al-Bahr and others. [3] His saying: 'due immediately' — meaning whether his right is due immediately or deferred, because deferment is for postponing demand, while the right is fully established, even if payment is not required immediately, as in an immediate debt. As in al-Fath. (1) In Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba (10:212) and others. (3) (2) From Ibn 'Abbas: A slave from the khums slaves stole from the khums, and that was raised to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and he did not cut him, and said: 'The wealth of Allah, some of it steals from some.' In Sunan Ibn Majah (2:864), Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq (10:212), and Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (8:283), and others. (3) Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (5:60).
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof regarding categories of property that, if stolen, do not incur the hadd penalty of cutting the hand. The matn (original text) lists three types: (1) public property (mal 'amma) like the treasury (Bayt al-Mal), (2) property in which the thief has a share (shirka), and (3) property that is the like (mithl) of a right owed to the thief, whether that right is due immediately or deferred. The commentary (sharh) explains each. For public property: because every Muslim has a right in it, creating a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd. Proof is given from the practice of 'Umar and 'Ali, and from a hadith of the Prophet regarding the khums (one-fifth of spoils). For shared property: the thief has ownership in it, so his share creates shubha. For the like of a right: if someone owes the thief money and the thief steals an equivalent amount, the hadd is not applied because the thief has a claim to that property, even if the debt is not yet due. The commentary further clarifies that similarity (mithliyya) can be actual or legal: for example, if the thief is owed dirhams and steals dinars, the two are considered one legal genus (jins wahid hukman) and thus no cut; but if he steals a different commodity (like jewelry), it is a different genus and the cut applies. Also, the deferment of a debt does not change the fact that the right is established, so stealing the like does not incur the hadd.

Key Terms
مال عامة (mal 'amma): Property that belongs to the general public, such as Bayt al-Mal (public treasury).
شركة (shirka): Partnership; property in which the thief has a share of ownership.
مثل حقه (mithl haqqihi): The like of the right that is owed to the thief; i.e., stealing an amount equivalent to a debt owed to him.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd penalties.
المثلية (al-mithliyya): The quality of being similar; here, it refers to fungible goods that can be substituted by like.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man is owed 100 dinars by another and steals 100 dinars from that person, is his hand cut?
A) Yes, because he stole property of another.
B) No, because he stole the like of his right, creating a shubha.
C) Only if the debtor was unwilling to pay.
D) Only if the debt was due immediately.

Key Takeaway
Shared ownership or a claim to the stolen property creates a shubha that drops the hadd, and this applies to public wealth, partnership property, and the exact equivalent of a debt owed.

Theft from Public and Shared Property: The Shubha that Drops the Hadd
Ruling on non-amputation for theft from public or shared property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three cases where theft from property does not incur the hadd of amputation according to the Hanafi school, and explain the shubha principle that drops the penalty.

Core Idea
When the thief has any right in the property, the hadd is dropped due to shubha.

This lesson examines a set of rulings from the Hanafi school regarding theft from public property, shared property, or property similar to a debt owed to the thief. The central principle is the presence of a doubt (shubha) arising from the thief's share or the public's right, which prevents the application of the fixed penalty of amputation. We will explore the proofs from the Companions and the Prophet (peace be upon him) that establish this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of non-amputation
The matn lists three cases: (1) theft from public property (mal 'ammah), (2) theft from property in which the thief has a share, and (3) theft of something similar to a right owed to the thief, whether immediate or deferred.
These are the specific scenarios where the hadd is dropped.
2
Understand the principle: shubha due to a right
The legal cause is that the thief has a right in the property, either as a member of the public (in bayt al-mal) or as a co-owner or creditor. This right creates a doubt (shubha) that removes the certainty required for the hadd.
Shubha is a broad concept in Hanafi law preventing hudud.
3
Examine the transmitted proofs (athar)
The 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah cites three proofs: (a) 'Umar's ruling regarding a theft from the treasury, (b) 'Ali's handling of a theft from spoils, and (c) the Prophet's statement about a slave who stole from the khums: 'The property of Allah steals from itself.'
These establish the precedent from the early generations.
4
Apply the legal genus (jins) to 'similar to his right'
The commentary clarifies that likeness (mithliyya) is determined by legal genus: gold and silver are one genus, so stealing dinars for a debt of dirhams still creates a shubha. Goods ('ard) are a separate genus, so theft of goods does not create a shubha.
This refinement prevents over-extension of the exception.
5
Recognize the scope: immediate and deferred debts
The ruling applies whether the debt is due now or later, because the right is fully established even if payment is delayed. The deferment only affects the demand, not the existence of the right.
The timing does not affect the shubha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومال عامة، ومال له فيه شركة، ومثل حقه حالاً، أو مؤجلاً.

قوله: ومال عامة؛ بالإضافة أي لا قطع بسرقة مال فيه حق عامة الناس؛ لأن لكل إنسان فيه حق من وجه، فيورث ذلك شبهةً أسقطت الحد، وقد أخرج ابن أبي شيبة: «إن رجلاً سرق من بيت المال، فكتب فيه سعد بن أبي وقاص إلى عمر فقال: ما من أحد إلا وله فيه حق لا قطع عليه». وأخرج عبد الرزاق: إن علياً أتى برجل سرق من المغنم، فقال: له فيه نصيب، وهو كائن فلم يقطعه. وفي «سنن ابن ماجة»: «إن عبداً سرق من الخمس، فلم يقطعه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وقال: مال الله يسرق بعضه بعضاً». ومن هنا يعلم وجه عدم القطع بسرقة مال مشترك بين السارق وبين ذي اليد، وبعدم بسرقة مال الوقف وبسرقة مثل حقه ممن له عليه حق.

قوله: ومثل حقه؛ أي مثله ظاهراً وإن اختلف قدراً وصفة، كما إذا سرق ما زاد على حقه أو سرق في الدين المؤجل، والمثلية أعم من أن تكون حقيقة أو حكماً، فإن كانت له دراهم فسرق دنانير، أو بالعكس لا يقطع؛ لأن النقدين جنس واحد حكماً بخلاف العرض، فإنه جنس آخر من كل وجه، ومنه الحلي؛ لأنه بسبب ما فيه من الصياغة التحق بالعرض كذا في «البحر» وغيره.

قوله: حالاً؛ أي سواء كان حقه حالاً أو مؤجلاً؛ لأن التأجيل لتأخير المطالبة، والحق ثابت كاملاً، وإن لم يلزم الإعطاء في الحال، كما في الدين الحال. كذا في «الفتح».
And public property, and property in which he has a share, and a like of his right, whether immediate or deferred. Commentary: His saying 'and public property' – by idafa – meaning no amputation for theft of property in which the general public has a right, because every person has a right in it from one perspective, so that creates a doubt that drops the hadd. Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated: 'A man stole from the treasury, so Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas wrote to 'Umar, and 'Umar said: 'There is no one but has a right in it, no amputation on him.' Abd al-Razzaq narrated that 'Ali was brought a man who stole from the spoils, and he said: 'He has a portion in it, he is such' and did not cut him. In Sunan Ibn Majah: 'A slave stole from the khums, and the Prophet did not cut him, saying: 'The property of Allah steals some of itself.' From this is known the reason for no amputation for theft of joint property between the thief and the possessor, and for no amputation for theft of waqf property, and for theft of a like of his right from one who has a right on him. Commentary: 'and a like of his right' – i.e., its apparent like even if different in amount and quality, as if he steals more than his right or steals in a deferred debt. The likeness is general whether real or legal: if he had dirhams and steals dinars, or vice versa, he is not cut because the two currencies are one genus legally, unlike goods, which are another genus from every aspect; including jewelry because due to its crafting it joins goods. So in al-Bahr and others. Commentary: 'immediate' – i.e., whether his right is immediate or deferred, because deferment is to delay the claim, and the right is fully established even if payment is not required immediately, as in an immediate debt. So in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a set of rulings regarding theft from property where the thief has a share or the public has a right. The commentator opens by stating that the matn lists three cases where amputation (hadd) is not applied: theft from public property (such as bayt al-mal), theft from property in which the thief has a share, and theft of something similar to a debt owed to the thief, whether the debt is due now or later. The underlying principle is the presence of a doubt (shubha) that prevents the hadd. Proofs are cited from the actions of the Companions: Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas wrote to 'Umar about a theft from the treasury, and 'Umar ruled no cut because everyone has a right in it. Similarly, 'Ali did not amputate a man who stole from the spoils of war because the thief had a share. A hadith from Ibn Majah reports that the Prophet did not cut off a slave who stole from the khums, saying 'The property of Allah steals from itself.' The commentary then clarifies the meaning of 'similar to his right': it includes cases where the thief's right is of the same genus (like gold and silver considered one genus legally) even if the specific items differ in amount or quality. If the thief steals something that is of a different genus (like goods instead of currency), amputation is applied because no shubha exists. Also, the timing of the debt (immediate or deferred) does not affect the ruling because the right is fully established.

Key Terms
مال عامة (mal 'āmmah): Public property, such as bayt al-mal (treasury) or spoils of war, where the general public has a right.
شبهة (shubhah): A doubt or ambiguity in the evidence of the crime that prevents the application of the fixed penalty (hadd).
مثل حقه (mithlu haqqih): Something that is similar to the right (debt) owed to the thief, whether in genus or legally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because every person has a right in it, creating a shubha
C) Because the thief is assumed to be poor
D) Because the amount is below the nisab threshold

Key Takeaway
The presence of any right (public or personal) in the stolen property creates a legal doubt (shubha) that prevents the application of the hadd for theft.

Theft of Excess from a Debtor
Ruling on theft of excess from a debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from a debtor when the stolen amount exceeds the debt, and distinguish when the shubha of partnership does not apply.

Core Idea
The thief's pre-existing right creates a shubha that suspends the hadd for the entire stolen amount, even if there is excess.

This lesson examines a specific hudud ruling: when a person steals from his debtor and the stolen amount exceeds the debt. The text clarifies that the hadd of amputation is not applied because the thief has a share in the property to the extent of his right, creating a legal doubt (shubha) that covers the entire theft. However, this protection does not extend to stealing from the debtor of a relative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act (stealing from one's own debtor) and what is the ruling? The text says no amputation is applied even if the stolen amount exceeds the debt.
The act must involve a pre-existing debt owed to the thief by the victim.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is the hadd suspended? Because the thief is a partner in the property to the extent of his right. This partnership creates a shubha (legal doubt) that blocks the hadd.
The shubha applies to the entire stolen amount, not just the portion equal to the debt.
3
Apply the extension
What if the stolen amount exceeds the debt? The same ruling applies: no amputation. The shubha covers the excess as well.
Similarly, if the thief steals something of better quality than what is owed, the ruling is the same.
4
State the limitation
When does the shubha not apply? If the theft is from the debtor of one's father or adult son, amputation is applied because the thief has no direct right in that debt.
The relationship must be direct: the debt must be owed to the thief himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بمزيد.

قوله: ولو بمزيد؛ الواو وصلية: أي ولو كانت السرقة ممن له حق عليه بأزيد من حقه؛ لأنه بمقدار حقه يصير شريكاً له، وكذا إذا سرق أجود من حقه، وهذا كله في السرقة من غريمه، فإن سرق من غريم أبيه أو غريم ولده الكبير يقطع.
And even if with an excess. Commentary: 'And even if with an excess' – the waw is connective: meaning even if the theft is from someone who has a right on him exceeding his right, because to the extent of his right he becomes a partner in it, and similarly if he steals something better than his right. All this applies when stealing from his own debtor. If he steals from the debtor of his father or the debtor of his adult son, he is cut.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This short passage explains an extension of the previous ruling. The matn states 'and even if with an excess,' meaning the ruling of no amputation applies even if the stolen amount exceeds the right (debt) owed to the thief. The commentary clarifies that the reason is that for the portion equal to his right, the thief becomes a partner in the property, creating a shubha for the entire theft. Similarly, if he steals something of superior quality (e.g., a better item than what is owed), the same principle applies. However, this is limited to theft from the thief's own debtor. If he steals from the debtor of his father or his adult son, the shubha does not exist because he has no direct right in that debt, so amputation is applied.

Key Terms
مزيد (mazīd): Excess amount over and above the right (debt) owed to the thief.
غريم (gharīm): Debtor; the person who owes a debt to the thief or to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is not cut because of the relationship
B) He is cut because he has no direct right in that debt
C) He is cut only if the amount exceeds his own right
D) He is not cut regardless of amount

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not amputate for theft from one's own debtor, even if the stolen amount exceeds the debt, due to the shubha of partnership.

Repeated Theft of the Same Item: The Hanafi Position on Amputation
Dispute on whether amputation is repeated for stealing the same item again

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether amputation is repeated for stealing the same unchanged item, identify the opposing evidence, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on hadith criticism and shubha.

Core Idea
A second amputation is not required for stealing the same unchanged item — shubha prevents the hadd from being repeated.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah examines a dispute about recidivism in theft. While one might assume that repeat theft always warrants a second amputation, the Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the stolen item remains unchanged. The discussion involves evaluating the strength of the opposing hadith and applying the principle of shubha (legal doubt) to suspend the punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core Hanafi ruling
Read the first part of the passage: 'he is not cut for stealing something for which he was cut once... while it had not changed from its state.' State the ruling clearly: no second amputation if the item is unchanged.
The key phrase is 'وهو بحاله' (while it is in its state).
2
Identify the opposing view and its evidence
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that amputation is repeated, citing the hadith 'If he returns, then cut him.' Locate this in the passage and note the two narrations (al-Daraqutni and Abu Dawud).
The opposing view is based on a general command to cut a repeat thief.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the hadith
The passage critiques the hadith: chain contains al-Waqidi (criticized), and al-Nasa'i declared it munkar. Understand that Hanafi method requires reliable evidence; a weak (da'if) or munkar hadith cannot override established principles.
This demonstrates usul al-hadith application in fiqh.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi debate on change of item
If the item changes physically (e.g., altered), later Hanafi sources say a second cut is applied. If the change is legal (e.g., owner sells then repurchases), the scholars of Bukhara say cut, Iraq say no cut. Note this nuance.
This shows the school's internal development beyond the founding imams.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position (Imam Muhammad) is no cut for unchanged item. Practically, a Hanafi judge would not order a second amputation in this case, while a Shafi'i judge would.
Thamarah al-khilaf: actual courtroom outcome differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قطع فيه، وهو بحاله.

قوله: بحاله؛ أي لا يقطع بسرقة شيء قطع فيه مرة، ثم وصل إلى مالكه، ثم سرقه والحال أنه لم يتغير عن حاله، وهذا عندنا. وأما عند أبي يوسف والشافعي رحمه الله يقطع؛ لقوله: «فإن عاد فاقطعوه». وهذا الحديث أخرجه الدارقطني في «سننه» بسند فيه الواقدي، متكلم فيه مرفوعاً: «إذا سرق السارق فاقطعوا يده، فإن عاد فاقطعوا رجله، فإن عاد فاقطعوا يده، فإن عاد فاقطعوا رجله». وأخرج أبو داود: «جيء بسارق إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقال: اقتلوه، فقالوا: يا رسول الله إنما سرق، قال: فاقطعوه فقطع، ثم جيء به الثانية فقال: اقتلوه، فقالوا: يا رسول الله إنما سرق، فقال: اقطعوه فقطع، وهكذا في الثالثة والرابعة، وفي الخامسة قال: اقتلوه. قال جابر: فانطلقنا به فقتلناه، ثم اجتررناه وألقيناه في البئر ورمينا عليه الحجارة». وفي سنده ضعف، والحديث منكر قاله النسائي.

قوله: بحاله؛ أما لو تغير قطع ثانياً، كما في «المجتبى»، ولو تغير لا بالذات، بل السبب كما إذا باعه المالك من السارق، ثم اشتراه منه فسرقه قطع ثانياً عند مشايخ بخارا، وقال مشايخ العراق: لا يقطع.
And what was cut [for] while it remains in its state. Commentary: 'On its state' – i.e., he is not cut for stealing something for which he was cut once, then it returned to its owner, then he stole it while it had not changed from its state. This is our position. As for Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on them, they say he is cut because of his statement: 'If he returns, then cut him.' This hadith was narrated by al-Daraqutni in his Sunan with a chain containing al-Waqidi, who is criticized, raised: 'If a thief steals, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot; if he returns, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot.' And Abu Dawud narrated: 'A thief was brought to the Prophet, he said: Kill him. They said: O Messenger of Allah, he only stole. He said: Cut him. So he was cut. Then he was brought a second time...' etc. Its chain is weak, and the hadith is munkar according to al-Nasa'i. Commentary: 'On its state' – if it changes, he is cut a second time, as in al-Mujtaba. If it changes not in essence but in cause, as when the owner sells it to the thief then buys it back, he steals it, then the scholars of Bukhara say he is cut a second time, and the scholars of Iraq say he is not cut.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute regarding repeated theft of the same item after it has been returned to the owner. The Hanafi school (Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds that if the item is unchanged (i.e., in the same state as when first stolen), no second amputation is required. The reason is that the original hadd already fulfilled the punishment for that specific act, and the item's return restores the original ownership, creating a shubha. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue that recidivism warrants a second cut, citing the hadith 'If he returns, then cut him.' The Hanafi response is that the hadith is weak (da'if) due to the presence of al-Waqidi in its chain and is also considered munkar by al-Nasa'i, so it cannot override the general principle. Additionally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute among later scholars: if the item changes (e.g., through alteration), a second cut is applied. If the change is not in the item itself but in the legal cause (e.g., the owner sells and repurchases it), the scholars of Bukhara say cut, while those of Iraq say no cut. This shows the nuanced application of the principle of shubha.

Key Terms
بحاله (bi-hālih): In its state; meaning the stolen item has not changed physically or legally since its return to the owner.
عاد (‘āda): He returns; refers to a thief who repeats theft after the hadd has been applied.
منكر (munkar): A hadith that is rejected due to a weak or suspect narrator; here, al-Nasa'i classifies the cited hadith as munkar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a second amputation not applied when a thief steals the same item a second time while it is unchanged?
A) Because the hadith cited by the opponents is weak
B) Because the original hadd has already been applied and shubha suspends the punishment
C) Because the thief has repented
D) Because the item is now owned by the thief

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not repeat amputation for theft of the same unchanged item because shubha and weak evidence prevent applying the hadd again.

Combining Amputation and Compensation for Theft
Dispute on coexistence of amputation and compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the coexistence of amputation and compensation for theft and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Amputation and compensation do not combine – the hadd precludes financial liability.

A key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools concerns whether a thief who has had his hand amputated still owes compensation for the stolen item. The Hanafis argue that the amputation itself invalidates the inviolability of the property for that thief, so no payment is due. The Shafi'is, however, see the two as separate penalties that can coexist. This lesson unpacks the proofs and the practical consequence of this ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: according to the Hanafis, amputation and guarantee (ḍamān) do not combine. The thief is not liable for compensation after the hadd.
The text uses 'عندنا' (according to us) to mark the school's stance.
2
Identify the Shafi'i argument
Recognize the threefold distinction made by al-Shafi'i: the two penalties differ in ruling (ḥukman), cause (sababan), and locus (maḥallan).
Amputation is for deterrence (right of Allah), compensation is for the victim's loss (right of the servant).
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Al-Shafi'i argues that since the Quranic command to cut off the hand does not mention negation of guarantee, the hadith 'no recompense after amputation' contradicts the clear text and should not be followed.
The Shafi'i school prioritizes the generality of the Quran over a specific hadith in this case.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis rely on the hadith as authentic and as specifying the general Quranic command. They also reason that the amputation lifts the inviolability (‘iṣma) of the stolen property, so the right to compensation is gone.
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that the penalty exhausts the financial liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical result: under Hanafi law, an amputated thief owes nothing for the stolen item. Under Shafi'i law, he must still compensate the victim even after amputation.
This difference affects both the victim's right to recovery and the thief's financial obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ 1 ] قوله : إن عصمة ... الخ ؛ قال ابنُ ملك في «شرح المنار» عند قول مؤلّف «المنار» في «بحث الخاص»: وبطلان عصمة المسروق بقوله حالة : جزاء لا بقوله : فَاقْطَعُوا ) (۳) ، اعلم أن القطعَ في السرقة مع الضمان لا يجتمعان عندنا. وقال الشافعي له : يجتمعان ؛ لأنهما مختلفان حكماً ؛ لأن الضمان يجبر المحل، والقطع للزجر، وسبباً ؛ لأن سبب القطع الجناية على حق الله ، وسبب الآخر الجناية على حق الغير، ومحلاً ؛ لأنَّ محلَّ أحدهما اليد، ومحل الآخر الذمة، فمن قال : القطع موجب لانتفاء الضمان ؛ لقوله : «لا غرم على السارق بعدما قطعت يمينه ، لم يكن عاملا بهذا الخاص، وهو قوله حالة : فَاقْطَعُوا .
[1] His saying: 'Indeed, the inviolability... etc.' Ibn Malik said in Sharh al-Manar, concerning the author of al-Manar's statement in the 'Research on the Specific' (Bahth al-Khas): 'And the invalidation of the inviolability of the stolen item by His statement 'a portion' [in the Quranic verse] not by His statement: 'then cut off [their hands]' (faqta'u).' Know that amputation in theft and guarantee do not coexist according to us (Hanafis). And Al-Shafi'i said: they coexist because they differ in ruling (hukman): guarantee compensates the loss, amputation is for deterrence; and in cause (sababan): the cause of amputation is transgression against the right of Allah, and the cause of guarantee is transgression against the right of another; and in locus (mahallan): the locus of one is the hand, and the locus of the other is the dhimma (financial liability). So whoever says that amputation necessitates the negation of guarantee, due to his statement: 'There is no recompense upon the thief after his right hand is cut off,' is not acting upon this specific [Quranic text], which is His statement: 'then cut off [their hands].'
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the coexistence of amputation (hadd) and financial compensation (damān) in theft. The commentator quotes Ibn Malik from Sharh al-Manar, explaining that according to the Hanafi school, amputation and guarantee do not combine (لا يجتمعان). The reason is that the amputation itself invalidates the inviolability (‘isma) of the stolen property for that thief, so he is not required to pay its value. The Shafi'i school, represented here by Al-Shafi'i, holds that they do combine. Al-Shafi'i's argument is that the two are different in three ways: (1) ruling (hukman): guarantee compensates the loss of the property, while amputation is a deterrent penalty; (2) cause (sababan): amputation is due to transgression against Allah's right (ḥaqq Allāh), while guarantee is due to transgression against the victim's right (ḥaqq al-‘abd); (3) locus (mahallan): amputation applies to the hand, while guarantee applies to the dhimma (financial liability). Al-Shafi'i then refutes the Hanafi proof – the hadith 'There is no recompense upon the thief after his right hand is cut off' – by arguing that this hadith, if authentic, would contradict the clear Quranic command to cut off the hand (which does not mention negation of guarantee). Thus, he says, the Hanafis are not acting upon the specific (al-khāṣṣ) of the Quranic verse. The passage ends with this retort. The Hanafi response, though not fully elaborated here, relies on the weakness of that hadith and the principle that amputation removes the obligation of compensation because the property's protection is nullified.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Financial liability to compensate for the stolen property, either by returning it or paying its value.
حالة (ḥālatun): In Quranic context (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:38), the word 'جزاء' (a portion) is interpreted by Hanafis as a reason for the ruling, not the command to cut off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, after a thief's hand is amputated for theft, is he still required to pay compensation for the stolen item?
A) No, compensation is not required because amputation precludes it.
B) Yes, compensation is required to repay the victim.
C) It depends on whether the item is recovered or not.
D) Compensation is waived only if the thief repents.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that amputation for theft removes the obligation of financial compensation, based on a hadith and the concept of inviolability.
